The Prohibition of Selling Human Milk: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sale of human milk

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling human milk and explain why Abu Yusuf's exception for slave women is rejected.

Core Idea
Human milk cannot be sold because the human is honored and milk is not property.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the sale of human milk, a question that arises in the context of wet nurses and medical treatment. The Hanafi school holds that human milk is not a valid commodity (mal) and therefore its sale is prohibited. However, Abu Yusuf made an exception for the milk of a slave woman, analogizing it to the sale of her person. The passage presents the Hanafi rebuttal and establishes the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The passage states that the human is honored (mukarram) and protected, so no part can be sold. This includes milk of both free and slave women. This is the position of Zahir al-Riwayah.
The term 'mukarram' is from Quran 17:70.
2
Locate the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf permits selling the milk of a slave woman by qiyas: just as her person can be sold, so can her part (milk).
This exception is specific to slave women; Abu Yusuf agrees on free women.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response distinguishes between the person (mahall for slavery) and milk, which lacks life and cannot be a locus for slavery or freedom. Therefore, the analogy (qiyas) fails.
The response uses the principle that milk has no life (hayah) and thus cannot be subject to the rulings of slavery.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If the sale occurs, the contract is fasid (voidable). The buyer does not own the milk, and if it is destroyed, no compensation (daman) is due because it is not considered property (mal).
This is the practical consequence that differentiates the two positions.
5
Apply the ruling to related issues
The passage also notes that destroying human milk does not incur liability, and using it as medicine for a sore eye is disputed: some prohibit, some permit if healing is known and no alternative exists.
This shows that the ruling is part of a broader framework about the inviolability of human body parts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هي جزء الآدمي وهو بجميع أجزائه مكرم مصون عن الابتذال بالبيع، أطلقه فشمل لبن الحرة والأمة وهو ظاهر الرواية، وعن أبي يوسف يجوز بيع لبن الأمة لجواز إيراد البيع على نفسها فكذا على جزئها . قلنا : الرق حل نفسها فأما اللبن فلا رق فيه لأنه يختص بمحل يتحقق فيه القوة التي ضده وهي الحي ولا حياة في اللبن فلا يكون محلاً للعتق ولا للرق فكذا البيع، فشمل ما إذا كان في إناء أو لا، والأولى أن يقيد مراده بما إذا كان في وعاء كما قيده في الهداية لأن حكم اللبن في الضرع قد تقدم، وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لا يضمن متلفه لكونه ليس بمال وإلى أنه لا يحل به التداوي في العين الرمداء وفيه قولان: فقيل بالمنع، وقيل بالجواز إذا علم فيه الشفاء. هكذا في فتح القدير هنا. وقال في موضع آخر : وأهل الطب يثبتون نفعاً للبن البنت للعين وهذه من أفراد مسألة الانتفاع بالمحرم للتداوي كالخمر. واختار في الخانية والنهاية الجواز إذا علم أن فيه الشفاء ولم يجد دواء غيره وسيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى تمامه في الحظر والإباحة وقيد بلبن المرأة لأنه يجوز بيع لبن الأنعام . قال الإمام الرباني محمد بن الحسن الشيباني : جواز إجارة الظئر دليل على فساد بيع لبنها وجواز بيع لبن الأنعام دليل على فساد إجارتها.
It is a part of the human, and the human with all his parts is honored, protected from being cheapened by sale. He (the author) made it general, so it includes the milk of a free woman and a slave woman, and that is the apparent riwayah. And from Abu Yusuf: the sale of a slave woman's milk is permitted, because it is permissible to sell her herself, so likewise her part. We said: Slavery dominates her person, but as for milk, there is no slavery in it because it is specific to a place where the opposite power is realized, which is life, and there is no life in milk, so it is not a place for 'itq or slavery, and likewise sale. So it includes whether it is in a vessel or not. It is better to restrict his intent to when it is in a container, as he restricted it in al-Hidayah, because the ruling of milk in the udder has preceded. And the author indicated that one who destroys it is not liable because it is not property, and that it is not permitted to treat a sore eye with it, and there are two sayings: one prohibits, and one permits if healing is known. Thus in Fath al-Qadir here. And he said elsewhere: The physicians affirm benefit of daughter's milk for the eye, and this is one instance of benefiting from forbidden things for treatment like wine. And al-Khaniyyah and al-Nihayah chose permission if healing is known and no other medicine is found. It will come, Allah willing, in al-Hadhr wa al-Ibahah. He restricted to woman's milk because sale of animal's milk is permitted. Imam al-Rabbani Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani said: The permissibility of hiring a wet nurse is evidence for the invalidity of selling her milk, and the permissibility of selling animal's milk is evidence for the invalidity of hiring it.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on the sale of human milk. The author discusses the ruling that human milk is not a valid commodity (mal) because the human is honored (mukarram) and cannot be cheapened by selling any part. The apparent riwayah (Zahir al-Riwayah) prohibits selling milk of both free and slave women, but Abu Yusuf permits selling the milk of a slave woman because her entire person can be sold. The Hanafi response is that slavery applies to the person, not to milk, as milk has no life and cannot be a locus for slavery or freedom. The author also notes that the milk is not considered property, so there is no guarantee (daman) for destroying it. There is a difference of opinion on using it as medicine for a sore eye: some prohibit, some permit if healing is known and no alternative. The passage ends with Shaybani's observation that the permissibility of hiring a wet nurse implies the prohibition of selling her milk, and vice versa for animals.

Key Terms
لبن المرأة (laban al-mar'ah): Human milk, considered not a fungible property because it originates from an honored human.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known transmitted ruling from the six books of Imam Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on selling the milk of a free woman?
A) Permissible (ja'iz)
B) Prohibited (haram)
C) Disliked (makruh)
D) Permissible only if in a vessel

Key Takeaway
Human milk is not property (mal) and cannot be sold; the human body and its parts are inviolable in commercial transactions.

Ruling on Sale of Pig Hair
Explains prohibition of selling pig hair but allows use for stitching due to necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the sale and use of pig hair and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding its impurity.

Core Idea
Pig hair is najis al-ayn; sale is prohibited to avoid honoring it, but use is permitted only for necessity.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the ruling on pig hair, which is considered impure by essence (najis al-ayn). The text explains why its sale is prohibited (haram) and why its use is restricted to necessity, such as stitching leather. It also presents an important intra-Hanafi disagreement between Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad regarding the impurity of pig hair, and settles on Abu Yusuf's view as the correct one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The core question: is pig hair impure (najis) or pure (tahir)? And what are the fiqh consequences for sale and use?
2
State the two Hanafi positions
Abu Yusuf holds pig hair is najis (impure). Muhammad holds it is tahir (pure). Each position leads to different rulings on sale, use, and taharah.
3
Analyze the proofs for each position
Examine the dalil for each: Abu Yusuf uses qiyas to the pig itself (najis al-ayn); Muhammad relies on the permissibility of use for necessity as evidence of purity.
The text mentions Ibn Sirin's practice not wearing khuffs stitched with pig hair as supporting Muhammad's view.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If pig hair is najis (Abu Yusuf), then water becomes impure if pig hair falls in, and cobblers' prayer with more than a dirham of pig hair on their clothes is invalid. If pure (Muhammad), neither of these consequences apply.
5
Identify the mufta bihi and its justification
The author declares 'al-sahih qawl Abi Yusuf' (the correct view is that of Abu Yusuf). The justification: the principle that what is established by necessity is proportionate to it; necessity does not change essential impurity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وشعر الخنزير أي لم يجز بيعه إهانة له لكونه نجس العين كأصله فالبيع هنا لو جاز لكان إكراماً وفي الخمر والخنزير كذلك لو جاز لكان إعزازاً وقد أمرنا بالإهانة، وفي لبن المرأة لو جاز لكان إهانة لها وقد أمرنا بإعزاز الآدمي فالفعل الواحد وهو البيع هنا يجوز أن يكون إعزازاً بالنسبة إلى محل وإهانة بالنسبة إلى آخر مثلاً إذا أمر السلطان بعض الغلمان بالوقوف عند الفرس بحضرته كان إعزازاً له، ولو أمر القاضي بذلك لكان إهانة له. وحاصله أن جواز بيع المهان إعزاز له وجواز بيع المكرم إهانة له قوله : (وينتفع به) أي يجوز الانتفاع بشعر الخنزير دفعاً لما يتوهم من منع بيعه ولكنه مقيد بالخرز للضرورة فإن ذلك العمل لا يتأتي بدونه ويوجد مباحاً فلا حاجة إلى القول يجواز بيعه وشرائه حتى لو لم يوجد لم يكره القاضي قوله : (كما قيده في الهداية أي حيث قال في قدح قال في النهر : وهذا القيد لبيان منع بيعه بعد انفصاله عن محله كيلا يظن أن امتناع بيعه ما دام في الضرع كغيره ؛ كذا في الفتح. وقال في الحواشي السعدية وهذا بعيد جداً بعدما تقدم أن بيع اللبن في الضرع لا يجوز اهـ. وبيانه أن امتناع بيعه في الضرع قد علم ما مر فذكر منع بيع لبن المرأة بعده نص في المنع بعد الانفصال فلا حاجة إلى التقييد به، وبه اندفع ما في البحر من أن ذكره أولى لأن حكم اللبن في الضرع قد تقدم على أنا لا نسلم أنه مستفاد مما تقدم بما قدمناه من أن الضرع خاص بذوات الأربع كالثدي للمرأة، وحينئذ فإنما أطلقه المصنف ليعم ما قبل الانفصال وما بعده. قوله : (ولكنه مقيد بالخرز للضرورة) هذا بناء على قول أبي يوسف بنجاسته ؛ أما على قول محمد الآتي من أنه طاهر فلا يتقيد الانتفاع به بالخرز ولا شراؤه للأساكفة للحاجة وكره بيعه لعدمها كما أفتى به الفقيه أو الليث، وظاهر كلامهم منع الانتفاع به عند عدم الضرورة بأن أمكن الخرز بغيره ولذا قيل : لا ضرورة إلى الخرز به لإمكانه بغيره. وكان ابن سيرين لا يلبس خفاً خرز بشعر الخنزير فعلى هذا لا يجوز بيعه ولا الانتفاع به ولذا روي عن أبي يوسف كراهة الانتفاع به إلا أن يقال إن إمكان الخرز بغيره وإن وقع لفرد بسبب تحمله مشقة في خاصة نفسه لا يجوز أن يلزم العموم حرجاً مثله، وحيث كان جواز الانتفاع به للضرورة والأصل أن ما ثبت للضرورة يتقدر بقدرها أفتى الإمام أبو يوسف بنجاسته فيتنجس الماء القليل إذا وقع فيه، وطهره محمد لأن جواز الانتفاع به دليلها، والصحيح قول أبي يوسف لما قدمناه وما ذكر في بعض المواضع من جواز صلاة الخرازين مع شعر الخنزير وإن كان أكثر من قدر الدرهم فهو مخرج على قول محمد بطهارته، وأما على قول أبي يوسف فلا وهو الوجه لأن الضرورة لم تدعهم إلى أن يعلق بحيث لا يقدرون بهم على الامتناع عنه ويجتمع على ثيابهم هذا المقدار
His statement: 'And pig hair' — i.e., its sale is not permitted, as a humiliation for it because it is impure in essence like its origin. For if sale were permitted, it would be an honor. Similarly for wine and pig, if permitted it would be an exaltation, and we are commanded to humiliate. And in the case of woman's milk, if permitted it would be a humiliation for her, and we are commanded to honor the human. So a single act, i.e. sale, may be an exaltation for one context and a humiliation for another. For example, if the sultan orders some slaves to stand by the horse in his presence, it is an honor for them; if the judge orders that, it is a humiliation. The gist is that permitting sale of something contemptible is an honor for it, and permitting sale of something honored is a humiliation for it. His statement: 'And benefit from it' — i.e., benefiting from pig hair is permitted, to remove the misconception that its sale is prohibited. But it is restricted to stitching for necessity, because that work cannot be done without it, and an alternative is available (i.e. lawful things), so there is no need to say its sale and purchase are permissible. Even if it were not available, the judge does not dislike it. His statement: 'As he restricted it in al-Hidayah' — i.e., where he said 'in a cup'. Al-Nahr said: This restriction is to clarify the prohibition of its sale after it is separated from its place, lest one think that its sale is forbidden only while in the udder like others? Thus in Fath. And al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyyah said this is very far-fetched after the preceding that the sale of milk in the udder is not permissible. Its explanation: the prohibition of sale in the udder is already known, so mentioning the prohibition of sale of woman's milk after it is explicit in the prohibition after separation, so there is no need for the restriction. Thus refutes what is in al-Bahr that mentioning it is better because the ruling of milk in the udder has preceded, but we do not concede that it is derived from the preceding, because the udder is specific to quadrupeds like the breast is for women. In that case, the author made it general to include before and after separation. His statement: 'But it is restricted to stitching for necessity' — this is based on Abu Yusuf's view of its impurity. As for Muhammad's view, that it is pure, then the benefit is not restricted to stitching, nor its purchase for cobblers for need, and sale is disliked in its absence, as the faqih or al-Layth gave fatwa. The apparent meaning from their statements is prohibition of benefiting from it when there is no need, i.e., if stitching can be done with something else. Hence it is said: there is no need to stitch with it because it can be done with something else. Ibn Sirin would not wear a khuff stitched with pig hair. On this basis, its sale and benefit are not allowed, and thus it is narrated that Abu Yusuf disliked benefiting from it, unless it is said that the possibility of stitching with something else, though it occurs for an individual due to hardship specific to himself, does not necessitate general hardship. And since the permissibility of benefiting from it is for necessity, and the principle is that what is established by necessity is proportionate to it, Imam Abu Yusuf gave fatwa on its impurity, so a small quantity of water becomes impure if it falls into it. Muhammad considered it pure because the permissibility of benefiting from it is evidence. The correct view is that of Abu Yusuf for what we have mentioned. And what is mentioned in some places about the permissibility of the cobblers' prayer with pig hair even if more than a dirham's amount is based on Muhammad's view of its purity. According to Abu Yusuf, it is not, and that is the correct view because necessity did not force them to hang it such that they cannot avoid it, and this amount collects on their clothes.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage delves into the ruling on pig hair, which is impure by essence (najis al-'ayn). Sale is prohibited because it would honor the pig, whereas we are commanded to humiliate it. The text uses an analogy: the same act of sale can be an honor or humiliation depending on the object. Benefit (use) is permitted only for necessity, specifically stitching leather, where no alternative exists. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the impurity of pig hair. Abu Yusuf deems it impure, so it cannot be used except for necessity, and it renders water impure. Muhammad considers it pure, allowing its use without restriction, and even purchase for cobblers is permissible but disliked without need. The author sides with Abu Yusuf, and notes that the ruling on cobblers' prayer with pig hair on their clothes (more than a dirham) is based on Muhammad's view; the correct view is that it prevents the validity of prayer.

Key Terms
نجس العين (najis al-'ayn): Impure in essence, like pig and wine; not just externally contaminated.
الخرز (al-kharz): Stitching leather, specifically with pig hair, permitted only for necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi), what is the ruling on pig hair when a small quantity falls into water?
A) The water becomes impure.
B) The water remains pure.
C) It depends on the amount.
D) It is only disliked, not impure.

Key Takeaway
Pig hair is najis al-ayn; sale is haram, and use is only permitted for necessity, but the impurity remains and affects taharah.

The Prohibition of Selling Human Hair
Prohibits sale and use of human hair, citing hadith on wasilah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling human hair and cite the hadith proof that establishes the prohibition.

Core Idea
Human hair cannot be sold because the human is honored (mukarram) and its dishonor is forbidden.

This passage addresses a common transaction: selling human hair. The Hanafi school firmly prohibits it, based on two strong foundations: the inherent honor of the human being and a prophetic hadith that curses those who engage in hair extension practices. In this lesson, we will examine the ruling, its rationale, and the specific evidence from the Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Determine what exactly is being ruled upon. The text states that both the sale (bay‘) and the benefit (intifa‘) of human hair are not permitted.
The prohibition covers both selling and using human hair for any purpose.
2
Understand the Rationale
The underlying reason is that the human being (ādamī) is honored (mukarram) and therefore none of his parts may be treated cheaply (mubadhdhal). This principle applies even if the person is a disbeliever.
The honor is inherent, not contingent on faith.
3
Locate the Proof
The author cites the hadith: 'La‘ana Allah al-wāṣilah wa al-mustawṣilah' (Allah cursed the hair extender and the one who requests it). This is a direct textual proof (dalīl naqlī).
The hadith is classified as ahad (single narration), but its meaning is clear.
4
Analyze the Implication
The hadith curses the act of attaching hair (wasl). Since selling human hair enables this prohibited act, the sale itself is also forbidden. This is an implicit analogy (dalālah iltizāmiyyah).
The connection between the cursed act and the sale is that sale facilitates the act.
5
Note the Concession
The text specifies that a concession (rukhsah) is given only for using animal fur (wabar) to add to women's hair. This highlights that the prohibition is specific to human hair.
The exception is for non-human materials.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وشعر الإنسان والانتفاع به) أي لم يجز بيعه والانتفاع به لأن الآدمي مكرم غير مبتذل فلا يجوز أن يكون شيء من أجزائه مهاناً مبتذلاً وقد قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم «لعن الله الواصلة والمستوصلة وإنما يرخص فيما يتخذ من الوبر فيزيد في قرون النساء وذوائبهن؛ كذا في الهداية. وصرح في فتح القدير بأن الآدمي مكرم وإن كان كافراً. والواصلة هي التي تصل الشعر بشعر النساء، والمستوصلة المعمول بها بأذنها ورضاها. ولعن في الحديث النامصة والمتنمصة والنامصة هي التي تنقص الحاجب لتزينه التي يفعل بها ذلك . والمتنمصة هي
His statement: 'And human hair and benefiting from it' — i.e., its sale and benefit are not permitted because the human is honored, not cheapened, so none of his parts may be dishonored and cheapened. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Allah cursed the wasilah and the mustawsilah, and concession is only given for what is taken from fur that is added to women's horns and locks [of hair].' Thus in al-Hidayah. And Fath al-Qadir stated explicitly that the human is honored even if he is a disbeliever. Al-wasilah is the one who attaches hair to women's hair, and al-mustawsilah is the one for whom it is done with her permission and consent. In the hadith, he also cursed the namisah and the mutanammisah. Al-namisah is the one who plucks the eyebrow to beautify it, the one who does it. And al-mutanammisah is she...
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. It prohibits both the sale and the use of human hair, because the human being is honored (mukarram) and cannot be cheapened. The hadith about the wasilah (hair extender) and the cursed actions of adding hair are cited as evidence. The text clarifies that even a disbeliever is honored in this context. Concession is only for using animal fur (wabar) to add to women's hair, not human hair. The passage also mentions related practices like plucking eyebrows (namisah), which are cursed.

Key Terms
الواصلة (al-wasilah): A woman who attaches hair to another woman's hair for beautification.
المستوصلة (al-mustawsilah): The woman on whom hair extension is done with her consent and permission.
الآدمي (al-ādamī): The human being, considered honored in Islamic law.
الانتفاع (al-intifā‘): Benefiting from something, using it for any purpose.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence cited for the prohibition of selling human hair?
A) The human is honored regardless of faith.
B) The hadith cursing the wasilah.
C) The impurity of hair.
D) The difficulty of cutting hair.

Key Takeaway
The sale and use of human hair is haram because humans are honored and the hadith curses hair extension practices.

Ruling on Dead Animal Skin and Other Parts Before and After Tanning
Explains prohibition of selling untanned dead animal skin but permits after tanning, and lists other parts that are property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling untanned dead animal skin and identify which parts of a dead animal are considered property (mal).

Core Idea
Untanned dead animal skin cannot be sold; after tanning it becomes saleable; bones, wool, etc. are pure property.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains the prohibition of selling the skin of a dead animal before tanning, based on a hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him). After tanning, the skin becomes pure and permissible to sell. The text also clarifies that other parts of the dead animal, such as bone, wool, nerve, horn, and fur, are considered property (mal) and can be sold, as they are inherently pure. Understanding this distinction is essential for proper application in trade and purity rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is selling the skin of a dead animal. Before tanning, the ruling is haram (prohibited) because the skin is impure and cannot be benefited from. After tanning, the ruling is halal (permissible) because tanning purifies it.
The hadith 'La tantafi'u min al-maytati bi ihab' (Do not benefit from a dead animal's skin) is the primary evidence.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is a hadith of the Prophet (peace be upon him): 'لا تنتفعوا من الميتة بإهاب' (Do not benefit from a dead animal's skin). This is a Sunnah ahad with zanni thubut, but its dalala (indication) is qati' because it explicitly prohibits benefit.
The term 'ihab' specifically refers to untanned skin.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition of benefiting from the skin indicates that it is not considered property (mal). Sale is a form of benefiting, so selling is also prohibited. After tanning, the skin becomes pure and permissible to benefit from; hence sale becomes permissible.
This shows the link between purity and saleability.
4
Extend to other parts of the dead animal
The author lists bone, wool, nerve, horn, and fur as property (mal). These are inherently pure and can be possessed, so they can be sold and benefitted from. This is supported by Zayla'i's comment that the general permission to benefit indicates purity.
This contrasts with the skin, which is impure before tanning.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi discussion on liability
The passage ends with a note that according to some narrations (non-Zahir al-Riwayah), these parts are not liable for compensation if destroyed. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that they are liable (maddaman) as per the text, but this needs verification.
This is a secondary issue; the main ruling on saleability stands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وجلد الميتة قبل الدبغ أي لم يجز بيعه لأنه غير منتفع به قال عليه السلام «لا تنتفعوا من الميتة بإهاب وهو اسم لغير المدبوغ فيكون نجس العين بخلاف الثوب والدهن المتنجس فإنها عارضة . قيد بما قبل الدبغ لأنه لو باعه بعده جاز لحل الانتفاع للطهارة ولذا قال : (وبعده يباع وينتفع به وقيد بالميتة لأن جلد المذكاة يجوز بيعه قبل الدباغة ولحوم السباع وشحومها وجلودها بعد الذكاة كجلود الميتة بعد الدبغ فيجوز بيعها والانتفاع بها ما عدا الأكل لطهارتها بالذكاة إلا جلد الخنزير قوله : (كعظم الميتة وصوفها وعصبها وقرنها ووبرها). أي المال وهو بالضرورة. قال الزيلعي في تعليل عدم إفساده الماء : إذا وقع فيه لأن إطلاق الانتفاع به دلیل طهارته اهـ. وهذا يقتضي جواز بيعه عند محمد أيضاً ولذا قال في النهر : وينبغي أن يطيب للبائع الثمن على قول محمد قوله : (لأن المال ما يمكن إحرازه الخ قال الرملي : عبارة الزيلعي ومحل البيع ما يمكن إحرازه وقبضه والهواء لا يمكن إحرازه. قوله : ( ولهذا يضمن بالإتلاف) قال الرملي : وفي شرح المجموع لابن ملك لا يضمن بالإتلاف فراجعه والظاهر أن ما هنا مخرج على غير ظاهر الرواية اهـ. قلت قال في النهر بعد نقل ما ذكره المؤلف عن الزيلعي : وأما تضمينه بالإتلاف بالمعنى الذي
His statement: 'And the skin of a dead animal before tanning' — i.e., its sale is not permitted because it cannot be benefited from. The Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Do not benefit from a dead animal's skin (ihab),' and ihab is the name for untanned skin, so it is impure in essence, unlike a garment or oil that becomes impure by contact, for that is incidental. He restricted it to before tanning because if he sells it after tanning, it is permissible due to the permissibility of benefiting from it after purification. Hence he said: 'And after tanning, it may be sold and benefited from.' He restricted it to dead animal because the skin of a properly slaughtered animal may be sold before tanning. The flesh of predatory animals, their fat, and their skins after proper slaughter (dhabh) are like the skins of dead animals after tanning: they may be sold and benefited from, except eating, due to their purity by proper slaughter, except pig skin. His statement: 'Like the bone of a dead animal, its wool, its nerve, its horn, and its fur' — i.e., they are property (mal) by necessity. Al-Zayla'i, in explaining why they do not spoil water: if it falls into water, because the absolute permission to benefit from it is evidence of its purity. This implies the permissibility of selling them according to Muhammad as well, hence al-Nahr said: It is appropriate that the seller be delighted with the price on Muhammad's view. His statement: 'Because property is what can be possessed, etc.' — al-Ramli said: Al-Zayla'i's expression is: 'The object of sale is what can be possessed and taken possession of, and air cannot be possessed.' His statement: 'And therefore it is liable for destruction' — al-Ramli said: In Sharh al-Majmu' by Ibn Malik, it is not liable for destruction, so check it. It appears that what is here is based on other than the apparent riwayah. I say: In al-Nahr after quoting what the author mentioned from al-Zayla'i: 'As for its liability for destruction in the sense that...'
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage rules on the skin of a dead animal (maytah) before and after tanning (dibagh). Before tanning, it is impure (najis al-'ayn) and cannot be sold or benefited from, based on the hadith prohibiting benefit from a dead animal's skin. After tanning, it becomes pure and can be sold and used. The text distinguishes from the skin of an animal slaughtered Islamically, which is pure without tanning. The author also lists other parts of a dead animal (bone, wool, nerve, horn, fur) that are considered property (mal) and can be sold and used, as they are pure by nature. There is a discussion about whether these parts render water impure: Zayla'i says they do not, because the general permission to benefit is evidence of purity. This supports Muhammad's view that these parts are pure and thus saleable. The passage ends with a note on liability for destruction: some say they are not liable (ghayr maddaman) according to non-Zahir al-Riwayah reports.

Key Terms
الدبغ (al-dibagh): Tanning process that purifies an impure skin.
إهاب (ihab): Untanned skin of a dead animal.
مال (mal): Property; something that can be possessed and has value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Permissible (ja'iz)
B) Prohibited (haram)
C) Permissible only if needed
D) Disliked (makruh)

Key Takeaway
The skin of a dead animal is impure and cannot be sold until tanned; its other parts (bone, wool, etc.) are pure and can be sold immediately.

The Ruling on Pig Hair: Necessity and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on pig hair impurity and necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on selling and benefiting from pig hair, and distinguish between the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani concerning its impurity.

Core Idea
Pig hair is impure (najis) according to the relied-upon Hanafi view; necessity permits its use but only to the extent required.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq delves into the legal status of pig hair, a substance that cobblers historically used for stitching. The text navigates the tension between necessity and impurity, presenting an internal Hanafi disagreement between the two great students of Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding this dispute illuminates the Hanafi method of balancing textual principles with practical needs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The passage rules that buying pig hair is allowed when needed (for cobblers), but selling it without need is disliked (makruh tahrimi). Also, benefiting from it is only permitted when there is no alternative.
The permissibility is tied to necessity, a key Hanafi principle.
2
Locate the intra-madhhab dispute
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ on whether pig hair is intrinsically impure (najis) or pure. Abu Yusuf holds it is impure; Muhammad holds it is pure.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
3
Examine the reasoning
Muhammad argues: the permissibility of benefiting from pig hair indicates its purity. Abu Yusuf counters: permissibility is due to necessity, which does not change the inherent impurity; what is established due to necessity is limited to its extent.
Both use the principle of necessity but reach different conclusions.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The text declares Abu Yusuf's view as sahih (correct) and the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).
Thus pig hair is legally impure in the Hanafi school.
5
Apply the practical consequence
If pig hair falls into a small amount of water, the water becomes impure according to Abu Yusuf (so cannot be used for wudu). According to Muhammad, it remains pure. The prayer of a cobbler with pig hair on his clothes is invalid if it exceeds a dirham in area according to Abu Yusuf, but valid according to Muhammad.
This shows the practical impact of the dispute on daily worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شراؤه للأساكفة للحاجة وكره بيعه لعدمها كما أفتى به الفقيه أو الليث، وظاهر كلامهم منع الانتفاع به عند عدم الضرورة بأن أمكن الخرز بغيره ولذا قيل : لا ضرورة إلى الخرز به لإمكانه بغيره. وكان ابن سيرين لا يلبس خفاً خرز بشعر الخنزير فعلى هذا لا يجوز بيعه ولا الانتفاع به ولذا روي عن أبي يوسف كراهة الانتفاع به إلا أن يقال إن إمكان الخرز بغيره وإن وقع لفرد بسبب تحمله مشقة في خاصة نفسه لا يجوز أن يلزم العموم حرجاً مثله، وحيث كان جواز الانتفاع به للضرورة والأصل أن ما ثبت للضرورة يتقدر بقدرها أفتى الإمام أبو يوسف بنجاسته فيتنجس الماء القليل إذا وقع فيه، وطهره محمد لأن جواز الانتفاع به دليلها، والصحيح قول أبي يوسف لما قدمناه وما ذكر في بعض المواضع من جواز صلاة الخرازين مع شعر الخنزير وإن كان أكثر من قدر الدرهم فهو مخرج على قول محمد بطهارته، وأما على قول أبي يوسف فلا وهو الوجه لأن الضرورة لم تدعهم إلى أن يعلق بحيث لا يقدرون بهم على الامتناع عنه ويجتمع على ثيابهم هذا المقدار
Its purchase by cobblers for need, and its sale is disliked when there is no need, as the jurist or al-Layth gave fatwa. And the apparent meaning of their speech is prohibition of benefiting from it when there is no necessity, if stitching can be done with something else. Hence it was said: There is no necessity for stitching with it because it is possible with something else. Ibn Sirin would not wear a khuff stitched with pig hair. So according to this, its sale and benefit are not permissible. Hence it is narrated from Abu Yusuf the dislike of benefiting from it, unless it is said that the possibility of stitching with something else, even if it occurs for an individual due to his bearing hardship in his own self, it is not permissible to impose a similar hardship on the generality. And since the permissibility of benefiting from it is due to necessity, and the principle is that what is established due to necessity is limited to its extent, Imam Abu Yusuf gave a fatwa of its impurity, so it renders a small amount of water impure if it falls into it. Muhammad declared it pure because the permissibility of benefiting from it is evidence of its purity. The correct is the saying of Abu Yusuf for what we have stated. And what is mentioned in some places about the permissibility of the prayer of cobblers with pig hair even if more than a dirham's amount is based on Muhammad's view of its purity. As for Abu Yusuf's view, it is not, and that is the proper view because necessity does not compel them to let it hang such that they cannot avoid it and this amount gathers on their clothes.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding pig hair. The passage explains that purchase of pig hair by cobblers is permitted due to necessity (for stitching), but selling it without necessity is disliked. The relied-upon view (al-sahih) is that of Abu Yusuf, who declares pig hair impure (najis), so if it falls into a small amount of water, it becomes impure. Muhammad al-Shaybani considers it pure because the permissibility of using it indicates purity. The practical difference affects the validity of prayer if a cobbler's clothes contain more than a dirham's amount of pig hair: according to Muhammad it is excusable, but according to Abu Yusuf it is not. The text reasons that necessity limits the permission, and the individual hardship does not justify a general relaxation.

Key Terms
الضرورة (al-darurah): Necessity that permits otherwise prohibited acts, limited to its extent.
نجاسة (najasah): Legal impurity affecting ritual purity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the passage, what is the ruling on pig hair falling into a small amount of water?
A) The water remains pure.
B) The water becomes impure.
C) The water becomes impure only if the pig hair is more than a dirham.
D) The water becomes pure and can be used for wudu.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules pig hair impure (najis); its use is allowed only to the extent of necessity, and the permission does not change its inherent impurity.

Prohibition of Selling and Using Human Hair
States the ruling and proof for prohibiting human hair sale and use.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling and using human hair and explain the proof from the hadith and the principle of human honor.

Core Idea
Human hair cannot be sold or used because humans are honored, not degraded.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq explains the ruling on human hair. It is forbidden to sell or benefit from human hair, based on the hadith cursing those who add hair extensions. The underlying principle is the sanctity and honor of the human body, which prohibits commodifying any part of it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The sale and use of human hair. What is the legal classification? It is haram (prohibited).
2
Understand the underlying principle
Why is it prohibited? Because a human being is honored (mukarram) and should not be degraded. This principle overrides any potential ownership rights.
The text explicitly states: 'al-adami mukarram'.
3
Cite the dalil (proof) from sunnah
The Prophet (peace be upon him) cursed the wasilah (woman who joins hair) and mustawsilah (woman who requests it). This indicates prohibition of using human hair for adornment.
The hadith is found in Bukhari and Muslim.
4
Note the exception for wool
A concession is given for using wool (not human hair) to add to women's hair. This highlights that the prohibition is specific to human hair due to human dignity.
Wool from animals is permissible.
5
Apply to related rulings
The passage also mentions the prohibition of plucking eyebrows (namis). This shows that altering body parts for beautification that degrades them is also prohibited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وشعر الإنسان والانتفاع به) أي لم يجز بيعه والانتفاع به لأن الآدمي مكرم غير مبتذل فلا يجوز أن يكون شيء من أجزائه مهاناً مبتذلاً وقد قال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم «لعن الله الواصلة والمستوصلة وإنما يرخص فيما يتخذ من الوبر فيزيد في قرون النساء وذوائبهن؛ كذا في الهداية. وصرح في فتح القدير بأن الآدمي مكرم وإن كان كافراً. والواصلة هي التي تصل الشعر بشعر النساء، والمستوصلة المعمول بها بأذنها ورضاها. ولعن في الحديث النامصة والمتنمصة والنامصة هي التي تنقص الحاجب لتزينه التي يفعل بها ذلك والمتنمصة هي
His saying: (And the hair of a human and benefiting from it) meaning its sale and benefiting from it are not permissible because a human is honored, not debased, so it is not permissible that any part of him be degraded and debased. And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'Allah has cursed the woman who joins hair (to others) and the woman who asks for it. And concession is only given for that which is taken from wool and added to women's locks and braids.' So it is in al-Hidayah. And it is stated explicitly in Fath al-Qadir that a human is honored even if he is a disbeliever. Al-wasilah is she who joins hair to women's hair, and al-mustawsilah is she upon whom it is done with her permission and consent. The hadith also curses al-namisah (the one who plucks eyebrows) and al-mutanammisah (the one who has it done). Al-namisah is she who plucks the eyebrows to beautify, and al-mutanammisah is she upon whom it is done.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on human hair: it is not permissible to sell it or benefit from it. The reason is that a human being is honored (mukarram) and should not be degraded. The text cites the hadith cursing women who add hair extensions and those who request them. However, a concession is noted for using wool (not human hair) to add to women's hair. The text also clarifies that the honor applies to all humans, including non-Muslims. Additional related prohibitions include plucking eyebrows (namis) for beautification.

Key Terms
الآدمي مكرم (al-adami mukarram): The human being is honored and should not be degraded.
الواصلة والمستوصلة (al-wasilah wa al-mustawsilah): The woman who adds hair and the woman who requests it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because it leads to wasting wealth.

Key Takeaway
The sale and use of human hair are prohibited because humans are honored and cannot be degraded.

Sale of Dead Animal Skin and Bones: Purification by Tanning
Clarifies permissibility of selling skin after tanning and bones/wool before tanning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling the skin of a dead animal before and after tanning, and explain why bones, wool, and similar parts are pure and saleable.

Core Idea
Dead animal skin is impure and unsaleable until tanned; bones and wool are always pure.

Islamic law strictly regulates benefiting from dead animals. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a key distinction: the skin of a dead animal becomes pure and saleable only after tanning, while parts that never had life—like bones and wool—are always pure. Understanding this rule is essential for correctly applying rulings on impurity and transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the legal status of selling dead animal skin before tanning? According to the Hanafi school, it is not permissible because the skin is impure (najis al-ayn) and cannot be used.
The proof is the hadith prohibiting benefit from the hide of a dead animal.
2
Distinguish before and after tanning
Tanning changes the ruling: after tanning, the skin becomes pure and can be sold and used. The text explicitly qualifies the ruling with 'before tanning' to highlight this difference.
This is based on the principle that tanning purifies the skin.
3
Compare with skin of slaughtered animal
The skin of an animal slaughtered Islamically (mudhakka) is pure from the start and can be sold even before tanning. This contrasts with the dead animal.
Slaughter (dhakah) purifies the animal.
4
Examine parts without life
Bones, wool, sinews, horns, and fur of a dead animal are considered pure because they never had life. Death does not affect them, so they can be sold and used without tanning.
The text lists these items as permissible.
5
Note exceptions and intra-madhhab differences
The elephant is treated differently: according to Muhammad, it is like the pig (najis al-ayn), but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?), it is like other predatory animals, so its bones can be sold. The monkey is permissible to sell according to the chosen view.
These details show the nuance within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وجلد الميتة قبل الدبغ أي لم يجز بيعه لأنه غير منتفع به قال عليه السلام «لا تنتفعوا من الميتة بإهاب وهو اسم لغير المدبوغ فيكون نجس العين بخلاف الثوب والدهن المتنجس فإنها عارضة . قيد بما قبل الدبغ لأنه لو باعه بعده جاز لحل الانتفاع للطهارة ولذا قال : (وبعده يباع وينتفع به وقيد بالميتة لأن جلد المذكاة يجوز بيعه قبل الدباغة ولحوم السباع وشحومها وجلودها بعد الذكاة كجلود الميتة بعد الدبغ فيجوز بيعها والانتفاع بها ما عدا الأكل لطهارتها بالذكاة إلا جلد الخنزير قوله : (كعظم الميتة وصوفها وعصبها وقرنها ووبرها). أي يجوز بيعها والانتفاع بها لأنها طاهرة لا يحلها الموت لعدم الحياة وقد قررناه من قبل، والفيل كالخنزير نجس العين عند محمد وعندهما بمنزلة السباع حتى يباع عظمه وينتفع به وجوز بيع القرد على المختار
His saying: (And the skin of the dead animal before tanning) means its sale is not permissible because it is not benefited from. He (peace be upon him) said: 'Do not benefit from the hide of a dead animal.' 'Ihab' is the name for untanned skin, so it is impure by essence (najis al-ayn), unlike a garment or oil that become impure, for that is accidental. It is qualified with 'before tanning' because if he sells it after tanning, it is permissible because benefit is allowed due to purity. Hence he said: (and after tanning it is sold and benefited from). It is qualified with 'dead animal' because the skin of an animal properly slaughtered (mudhakka) may be sold before tanning. And the flesh of predatory animals, their fats, and their skins after slaughter are like the skins of dead animals after tanning: their sale and benefit are permissible except eating, because they become pure by slaughter, except the skin of the pig. His saying: (like the bone of the dead animal, its wool, its sinew, its horn, and its fur) means their sale and benefit are permissible because they are pure; death does not affect them since there was no life. And we have established this before. The elephant is like the pig: impure by essence according to Muhammad, but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?) it is like predatory animals, so its bone may be sold and benefited from. The sale of a monkey is permitted according to the chosen view.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage covers two related rulings. First, the skin of a dead animal (maytah) cannot be sold before tanning because it is impure (najis al-ayn) and cannot be used. Proof: the hadith prohibiting benefit from the hide of a dead animal. After tanning, it becomes pure and can be sold and used. In contrast, the skin of an animal slaughtered Islamically (mudhakka) can be sold even before tanning. Second, parts of a dead animal that did not have life—like bones, wool, sinews, horns, and fur—are pure and can be sold and used. The passage notes an exception: the pig is impure by essence according to Muhammad, but according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?) it is like other predatory animals, so its bones can be sold. The monkey is treated as permissible to sell according to the chosen view.

Key Terms
نجس العين (najis al-ayn): Essentially impure, cannot be purified by washing.
إهاب (ihab): Untanned skin.
ذكاة (dhakah): Islamic slaughter that purifies the animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Both before and after tanning.
B) Only after tanning.
C) Only before tanning.
D) Never, regardless of tanning.

Key Takeaway
Tanning transforms impure dead animal skin into pure, saleable leather; bones and wool are always pure.

Selling the Upper Storey After Collapse: Why the Right of Support Is Not Property
Ruling with proof on invalidity of sale after collapse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling the upper storey after collapse and explain why haqq al-ta'li is not considered property (mal).

Core Idea
A collapsed upper storey leaves only a right of support, which is not property—thus its sale is batil.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the commentator explains a key Hanafi distinction: not everything that has value can be sold as property. After a building collapses, the owner of the upper storey retains only a right to have it supported, but that right is not considered mal (property) in the Hanafi school. Hence, selling it is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
The ruling concerns the sale of an upper storey (علو) after it has collapsed. Determine that the subject of sale is not the building itself (which is gone), but the remaining right of support.
2
Locate the reasoning
The commentator states that after collapse, only haqq al-ta'li (right of support) remains, and it is not mal (property). Understand that the invalidity stems from the absence of a saleable object.
3
Understand the Hanafi definition of mal
Property (mal) is defined as something that can be possessed and has physical existence. Haqq al-ta'li is a mere right, not a tangible asset, so it falls outside the definition.
Contrast with water rights (sharb), which are considered property because they involve water—a tangible substance.
4
Note the temporal condition
The sale before collapse is valid because the building itself is the subject. After collapse, only a right remains, thus the sale becomes batil.
This is from Fath al-Qadir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وعلو سقط) أي لم يجز بيع علو بعد انهدامه لأن الباقي بعد سقوطه حق التعلي وهو ليس بمال لأن المال ما يمكن إحرازه والمال هو المحل المبيع بخلاف الشرب حيث يجوز بيعه تبعاً للأرض باتفاق الروايات، ومفرداً في رواية وهو اختيار مشايخ بلخ لأنه الماء ولهذا يضمن بالإتلاف وله قسط من الثمن وسيأتي تمامه في الشرب إن شاء الله تعالى. وقيد بسقوطه لأن بيعه قبل سقوطه جائز كما في فتح القدير لأن المبيع البناء فعلى هذا بيع سقف البيت قبل نقضه كما يجوز بيع البناء قبل هدمه لكن في عمدة الفتاوى : لا يجوز بيع بناء الوقف قبل هدمه ولا الأشجار الموقوفة المثمرة قبل قلعها بخلاف غير المثمرة اهـ. وأشار المصنف إلى أن العلو لو سقط قبل القبض فإن البيع يبطل كهلاك المبيع قبل القبض كما في فتح القدير والعلو خلاف السفل بضم العين وكسرها ؛ كذا في المصباح. ولم يذكر الطريق والمسيل. وفي الهداية : بيع الطريق وهبته جائز، وبيع وبيع باطل، والمسألة تحتمل وجهين : بيع رقبة الطريق والمسيل وبيع حق المرور والتسييل؛ فإن كان المراد الأول فهو الفرق بين المسألتين أن الطريق معلوم لأن له طولاً وعرضاً معلوماً، أم المسيل فمجهول لأنه لا يدري قدر ما يشغله من الماء. وإن كان الثاني ففي بيع حق المرور روايتان، وجه الفرق على أحدهما بينه وبين حق التسييل أن حق المرور معلوم لتعلقه بمحل معلوم وهو الطريق، وأما المسيل على السطح فهو حق التعلي وعلى الأرض مجهول لجهالة محله . . ووجه الفرق بين حق المرور وحق التلعي على إحدى الروايتين أن حق التعلي يتعلق بعين لا تبقى وهو البناء فأشبه المنافع، أما حق المرور يتعلق بعين تبقى وهو الأرض فأشبه الأعيان اهـ .
His statement: (and the upper storey that collapses) meaning: it is not permissible to sell an upper storey after it has collapsed because what remains after its collapse is the right of support (haqq al-ta'li), and it is not property because property is that which can be possessed, and property is the sold item. This is contrary to water rights (sharb) where selling it is permissible as a subsidiary of the land by agreement of all narrations, and individually in one narration, which is the choice of the scholars of Balkh, because it is water, and due to this it is liable for compensation when destroyed, and it has a share of the price. Its completion will come in the section on water rights, God willing. He restricted it to its collapse because selling it before its collapse is permissible, as in Fath al-Qadir, because the sold item is the building. Accordingly, selling the roof of a house before dismantling it is like selling a building before demolition. However, in 'Umdat al-Fatawa: it is not permissible to sell a building of a waqf before its demolition, nor trees of a waqf that bear fruit before uprooting, unlike non-fruit-bearing trees. End. The author indicated that if the upper storey collapses before delivery, the sale is void like the destruction of the sold item before delivery, as in Fath al-Qadir. 'Uluww is the opposite of 'sifl' with damm or kasr of 'ayn, as in al-Misbah. He did not mention the road and watercourse. In al-Hidayah: selling a road and gifting it is permissible, and selling [the right of] watercourse is impermissible. The issue has two aspects: selling the substance (raqaba) of the road and watercourse, and selling the right of passage and water flow. If the first is intended, then the difference between the two issues is that the road is known because it has a known length and width, while the watercourse is unknown because one does not know the amount of water that occupies it. If the second, then regarding selling the right of passage there are two narrations. The basis of the difference on one of them between it and the right of water flow is that the right of passage is known because it is attached to a known place, the road. As for the watercourse on the roof, it is the right of support, and on the ground it is unknown due to the unknown location. And the basis of the difference between the right of passage and the right of support on one of the two narrations is that the right of support is attached to a substance that does not remain, the building, thus it resembles usufructs. As for the right of passage, it is attached to a substance that remains, the ground, thus it resembles physical objects. End.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the sale of the upper storey (علو) of a building after it has collapsed. The commentator explains that the sale is impermissible because what remains after the collapse is merely a right of support (حق التعلي), which is not considered property (مال) in the Hanafi school. Property is defined as something that can be possessed and transferred, and the right of support does not meet that definition. This is contrasted with water rights (شرب), which are considered property and can be sold, either as part of the land or independently in one narration. The ruling is restricted to after collapse; before collapse, the sale is valid because the building itself is the object of sale, as stated in Fath al-Qadir. The passage also discusses the distinction between selling a road (طريق) and a watercourse (مسيل) based on certainty of description. The road has known dimensions, so its sale is valid, while the watercourse is uncertain in volume, so its sale is invalid. However, if the sale pertains to the right of passage or water flow, there are two narrations, with the right of passage being like a usufruct and the right of water flow on a roof being a right of support, which is not property. The key takeaway is that only tangible property (مال) can be sold, and rights that are not property cannot be transferred for a price.

Key Terms
علو (‘uluww): Upper storey of a building; the subject of sale discussed in this passage.
حق التعلي (haqq al-ta‘li): Right of support; the claim to have one's structure supported by another's property, considered a non-property right.
مال (māl): Property; defined as something that can be possessed and has value according to custom.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on selling the upper storey of a building after it has collapsed?
A) The sale is valid because the upper storey is part of the building.
B) The sale is valid if both parties agree.
C) The sale is invalid because only the right of support remains, which is not property.
D) The sale is makruh but not invalid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of property (mal) excludes rights like haqq al-ta'li, making their sale invalid.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

The Validity of Selling a Road vs a Watercourse: Certainty in Descriptions
Expansion on the distinction between road and watercourse with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the sale of a road and a watercourse based on the Hanafi principle that the subject of sale must be known (ma'lum), and apply the two-narration rule for rights of passage.

Core Idea
A road is a known entity, so its sale is valid; a watercourse is uncertain, so its sale is invalid unless sold as a right with known location.

In the same passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the commentator turns to a related distinction: why selling a road is valid but selling a watercourse is invalid. He analyzes two possible interpretations—whether the sale is of the physical substance or of the right—and discusses the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on selling rights of passage. This lesson unpacks that comparison and the role of certainty (ma'lumiyyah) in sale contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possible interpretations
The commentator clarifies that the issue of selling a road and watercourse can be understood in two ways: either as selling the physical substance (raqaba) or as selling the right of passage/water flow.
2
If sale of substance: compare certainty
If selling the substance, the road has known length and width, making it ma'lum (known). The watercourse is unknown in terms of the amount of water it carries, hence there is gharar (uncertainty) and the sale is invalid.
This is the more straightforward view.
3
If sale of right: examine the two narrations
If selling the right of passage (haqq al-marrur) vs right of water flow (haqq al-tas'il), there are two narrations. On one narration, both are saleable; on the other, right of passage is valid but right of water flow is not, because the latter is like haqq al-ta'li (right of support).
The difference hinges on whether the right attaches to a permanent or non-permanent entity.
4
Understand the analogy
The commentator explains that haqq al-ta'li attaches to a non-permanent entity (the building), thus resembles usufructs and is not property. Haqq al-marrur attaches to the permanent ground, thus resembles physical objects and is property.
This analogy clarifies why one right is saleable and the other is not.

Key Terms
طريق (ṭarīq): Road; a defined path with known length and width.
مسيل (masīl): Watercourse or drain; an undefined channel for water.
حق المرور (haqq al-murūr): Right of passage; the right to cross another's land.
حق التسييل (haqq al-tasīl): Right of water flow; the right to discharge water onto another's property.

Key Takeaway
A sale contract requires the object to be known (ma'lum); uncertainty in description invalidates the sale, as seen in the contrast between a road (known) and a watercourse (unknown).

Sale of a Slave Misidentified by Gender: The Principle of Indication and Naming
Ruling on sale of slave whose gender is mistaken, with the principle of indication and naming

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a slave's gender is misidentified in a sale, explain the principle of indication and naming (al-ishara ma'a al-tasmiya) of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, and apply the definition of jins in fiqh to determine whether a contract is void or subject to khiyar.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, selling a slave of the wrong gender voids the contract because male and female humans are different jins; this rests on Muhammad's principle of indication and naming.

Imagine purchasing a slave-girl only to discover you have received a male slave—does the sale stand? The Hanafi school rules it void, not merely voidable, because of how it categorises the 'jins' (category) of the object. This seemingly technical ruling is built upon a foundational principle of contract law attributed to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani: the rule of 'indication and naming' (al-ishara ma'a al-tasmiya). In this lesson, we unpack the ruling, the principle, and the unique Hanafi definition of jins that governs sales and many other contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its trigger
The buyer specifies a slave-girl but receives a male slave. The Hanafi ruling: the sale is batil (void). Why? Because male and female are different jins in fiqh.
Contrast with animals: a ram turning out to be a ewe gives a valid sale with khiyar, because animal genders are same jins.
2
Understand the principle of indication and naming
When a contract both indicates an object (by pointing) and names it, the contract attaches to the named item if the indicated and named items are of different jins, leading to voidness due to non-existence of the specified object. If same jins, it attaches to the indicated item, valid with khiyar for misdescription.
This principle applies to all contracts: marriage, hire, khul', etc., as stated by Imam Muhammad.
3
Apply the definition of jins in fiqh
Jins in fiqh is defined by similarity of purpose (gharad). Two items are same jins if purposes do not differ greatly; different jins if they do. Human genders are different jins; animal genders are same jins. Also, vinegar and molasses are different jins despite both being condiments.
This differs from the logical definition of genus (jins dhati) which focuses on essential attributes.
4
Explore the internal dispute on khiyar
When the actual item is of the same jins but superior quality (e.g., writer instead of baker), the majority of Hanafis (including the author of al-Hidaya) hold that the buyer has khiyar. Some later scholars (Sadr al-Islam, Zahir al-Din) deny khiyar by analogy to buying a slave described as disbeliever and finding him Muslim.
The dispute hinges on whether the purpose is lost: a baker is needed for baking, not writing, whereas a Muslim slave can serve any purpose a disbeliever could.
5
Trace the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under the majority view, a buyer who specifies 'baker' and receives a 'writer' can return the slave. Under the minority view, he cannot. The majority view is the mufta bihi (operative position for fatwa).
This illustrates how a subtle difference in reasoning (consideration of buyer's purpose) leads to a different practical outcome.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وأمة تبين أنه عبد وكذا عكسه) أي لم يجز بيع أمة ظهر أنه عبد وعكسه وهو بيع عبد تبين أنه جارية بخلاف ما إذا باع كبشاً فإذا هو نعجة حيث ينعقد البيع ويتخير والفرق ذكره الشارح فهو إحدى الروايتين والفتوى على أنه لا يضمن كما في الذخيرة. وفي الظهيرية : وهو الأصح. وعن الشيخ جلال الدين ابن صاحب الهداية أنه قصر ضمانه بالإتلاف على ما إذا شهد به الآخر ثم رجع بعد القضاء وقال : لا وجه للضمان بالإتلاف إلا بهذه الصورة لأنه لو ضمن بغيرها فإما بالسقي أو بمنع حق الشرب لا وجه للأول لأن الماء مشترك بين الناس ولا إلى الثاني لأن منع حق الغير ليس سبباً للضمان بل السبب منع ملك الغير ولم يوجد ؛ كذا في الفتح. قوله: (قيد بسقوطه الخ) قال في الفتح فرع : باع العلو قبل سقوطه جاز فإن سقط قبل القبض بطل البيع لهلاك المبيع قبل القبض .اهـ. وفي الخانية : رجل له علو وسفل فقال لرجل بعت منك علو هذا السفل بكذا جاز البيع ويكون سطح السفل لصاحب السفل وللمشتري حق القرار، وكذا لو انهدم هذا العلو كان للمشتري أن يبني عليه علواً آخر مثل الأول لأن السفل اسم لمبنى مسقف فكان سطح السفل سقفاً للسفل اهـ . فتأمله مع قول المؤلف «لأن المبيع البناء قوله : كذا ذكر المصنف) أي صاحب الهداية . قوله : (بما لا يليق به أي بالسيد تأمل .
باب البيع الفاسد ١٣٥ جيم اتحاد يبتني على الأصل الذي ذكرناه في النكاح لمحمد وهو أن الإشارة مع التسمية إذا اجتمعتا ففي مختلفي الجنس يتعلق العقد بالمسمى ويبطل لانعدامه، وفي متحدي الجنس يتعلق بالمشار إليه وينعقد لوجوده ويتخير لفوات الوصف كمن اشترى عبداً على أنه خباز فإذا هو كاتب وفي مسألتنا الذكر والأنثى من بني آدم جنسان للتفاوت في الأغراض وفي الحيوانات جنس واحد للتقارب فيها وهو المعتبر دون الأصل كالخل والدبس جنسان والوذاري والزنديجي على ما قالوا جنسان مع اتحاد أصلهما؛ كذا في الهداية. والأصل المذكور لمحمد رحمه الله تعالى متفق عليه هنا ويجري في سائر العقود من النكاح والإجارة والصلح عن دم العمد والخلع والعتق على مال والبيع في مسألة الكتاب باطل لعدم المبيع، وبه ظهر أن الذكر والأنثى من بني آدم جنسان فقهاً وإن اتحدا جنساً في المنطق لأنه الذاتي المقول على كثيرين مختلفين بمميز داخل، والجنس في الفقة المقول على كثيرين لا يتفاوت الغرض منها فاحشاً فالجنسان ما يتفاوت الغرض منهما فاحشاً بلا نظر إلى الذاتي والوذاري بفتح الواو وكسرها وإعجام الذال ثم راء مهملة نسبة إلى وذار قرية من قرى سمرقند والزنديجي بزاي ثم نون ثم دال مهملة ثم ياء ثم نسبة إلى زندنه بفتح الزاي والنون الأخيرة والجيم زيدت على خلاف القياس مع أصلهما ؛ هكذا ذكر صاحب الهداية عن المشايخ . قال في فتح القدير: ومن المختلفي الجنس ما إذا باع أنه ياقوت فإذا هو زجاج فالبيع باطل، ولو باعه ليلاً على أنه ياقوت أحمر فظهر أصفر صح ويخير كما إذا باع عبداً على أنه خباز فإذا هو كاتب؛ هكذا ذكر المصنف وإن كانت صناعة الكتابة أشرف عند الناس من الخبز وكان المصنف ممن لا يفرق من المشايخ بين كون الصفة التي ظهرت خيراً من الصفة التي عينت أولاً في ثبوت الخيار كما أطلق في المحيط ثبوت الخيار. وذهب آخرون منهم صدر الإسلام وظهير الدين إلى أنه إنما يثبت إذا كان الموجود أنقص وصحح الأول لفوات غرض المشتري وكان مستند المفصلين ما تقدم فيمن اشترى عبداً على أنه كافر فإذا هو مسلم لا خيار له لأنه خير مما عين. وقد يفرق بأن الغرض وهو استخدام العبد بما لا يليق به لا يتفاوت بين مسلم وكافر من الزراعة وأمورها أو التجارة وأمورها بخلاف تعيين الخبز أو الكتابة فإنه يفيد أن حاجته التي لأجلها اشترى هي هذا الوصف اهـ. وقد ظهر من كلامهم أن من اشترى فصوصاً ثم اختلفا قال المشتري شرطت لي ياقوتاً وأنكره البائع أنه إن كان ما ظهر من خلاف جنس الياقوت تحالفاً وفسخ البيع لأن الاختلاف في جنس المبيع، وإن كان ما ظهر من جنسه وإنما الفائت الوصف، فإن للمشتري أن يبني عليه علواً آخر مثل الأول لأن السفل اسم لمبنى مسقف فكان سطح السفل سقفاً للسفل اهـ . فتأمله مع قول المؤلف «لأن المبيع البناء قوله : كذا ذكر المصنف) أي صاحب الهداية . قوله : (بما لا يليق به أي بالسيد تأمل .
His statement: (and a slave-girl who turns out to be a male slave, and likewise the opposite) meaning: it is not permissible to sell a slave-girl who appears to be a male slave, and the opposite, which is selling a male slave who turns out to be a female slave. This is unlike the case of selling a ram and it turns out to be a ewe, where the contract is valid and he has an option (khiyar). The difference was mentioned by the commentator: it is one of the two narrations, and the fatwa is that he is not liable for compensation, as in al-Dhakhira. In al-Zahiriyya: it is the most correct. From Shaykh Jalal al-Din ibn Sahib al-Hidaya: he restricted liability for destruction to the case where the other witness testifies and then retracts after judgment, saying: there is no basis for liability for destruction except in this form, because if he were liable otherwise, it would be either due to watering or preventing the right to water. The first has no basis because water is shared among people, and the second has no basis because preventing another's right is not a cause for liability; the cause is preventing another's property, and that did not occur. Thus in al-Fath. His statement: (restricted to its collapse, etc.) He said in al-Fath: a branch: if one sells the upper storey before its collapse, it is permissible; if it collapses before delivery, the sale becomes void due to destruction of the sold item before delivery. End. And in al-Khaniyya: a man has an upper storey and a lower storey; he said to a man: I sold you the upper storey of this lower storey for such-and-such; the sale is valid, and the surface of the lower storey belongs to the owner of the lower storey, and the buyer has the right of settlement. Similarly, if this upper storey collapses, the buyer may build another upper storey like the first, because the lower storey is the name for a roofed building; so the surface of the lower storey is the roof of the lower storey. End. Consider it with the author's statement: 'because the sold item is the building.' His statement: (thus mentioned by the author) i.e., the author of al-Hidaya. His statement: (with what is not befitting him) i.e., the master. Consider.
Chapter of Invalid Sale, 135, Jīm Ittiḥād: It is based on the principle we mentioned in marriage according to Muhammad: that when indication and naming are together, in cases of different jins (categories) the contract is attached to the named item and becomes invalid due to its non-existence; in cases of the same jins, it is attached to the indicated item and is valid due to its existence, and he has an option due to loss of description, like one who buys a slave on the condition that he is a baker and finds him to be a writer. In our case, the male and female of human beings are two jins due to significant difference in purposes, while in animals they are one jins due to closeness, and this is what is considered, not the [logical] genus. Thus vinegar and molasses are two jins, and Wadhari and Zandiji are said to be two jins despite their common origin. Thus in al-Hidaya. The aforementioned principle of Muhammad (may God have mercy on him) is agreed upon here and applies in all contracts: marriage, hire, reconciliation for intentional killing, khul', manumission for money, and sale. In the case of the book, the sale is invalid due to non-existence of the sold item. It becomes clear that male and female of humans are two jins in fiqh even if they are one jins in logic, because [the logical] genus is essential (dhati) predicated on many differing by an internal differentia, while jins in fiqh is predicated on many where the purpose does not differ greatly. So two jins are those where the purpose differs greatly, without regard to the essential. Wadhari: with fath or kasr of waw and i'jam of dhal, then ra' important, relative to Wadhār, a village of Samarqand. Zandiji: with zay, then nun, then dal important, then ya', relative to Zandana with fath of zay and nun, the last nun, and jim added contrary to analogy with its origin. Thus mentioned the author of al-Hidaya from the scholars. He said in Fath al-Qadir: Among different jins is if one sells on the condition that it is a ruby and it turns out to be glass, the sale is invalid. If he sells it at night on the condition that it is a red ruby and it appears yellow, the sale is valid and he has an option, as when one buys a slave on the condition that he is a baker and finds him to be a writer. Thus the author mentioned, even though the occupation of writing is more honorable in people's eyes than baking. The author is among those who do not differentiate among the scholars between whether the attribute that appears is better than the attribute specified initially in establishing the option, as al-Muhit asserts the option absolutely. Others, such as Sadr al-Islam and Zahir al-Din, held that it is only established if the found attribute is inferior; they preferred the first due to loss of the buyer's purpose. Their basis is the precedent of one who buys a slave on the condition that he is a disbeliever and finds him to be a Muslim: he has no option because he is better than specified. And it can be distinguished that the purpose, which is employing the slave in what is not befitting him, does not differ between Muslim and disbeliever in agriculture and its like or trade and its like, unlike specifying baking or writing, as that indicates his need for that specific description. End. It has become clear from their statements that if one buys rubies (fusus) and then they disagree, the buyer saying 'I stipulated ruby' and the seller denying, then if what appeared is of a different jins from ruby, they take an oath and the sale is rescinded because the difference is in the jins of the sold item. If what appeared is of its jins and only the description is lost, then the buyer may build another upper storey like the first, because the lower storey is the name for a roofed building; so the surface of the lower storey is the roof of the lower storey. End. Consider it with the author's statement 'because the sold item is the building.' His statement: (thus mentioned by the author) i.e., the author of al-Hidaya. His statement: (with what is not befitting him) i.e., the master. Consider.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This lengthy passage covers the ruling on selling a slave whose gender is mistakenly identified. The key principle is that in humans, male and female are considered two different jins (categories) in Hanafi fiqh, because the purposes (aghdad) for which they are purchased differ significantly. Therefore, if a buyer purchases a slave-girl but receives a male slave (or vice versa), the contract is void (batil) because the object of sale does not exist. However, if one buys a ram and finds it to be a ewe (or vice versa), the contract is valid because in animals, male and female are the same jins due to similarity in purpose; the buyer has an option (khiyar al-wasf) due to the missing description. The passage then discusses a subsidiary issue: liability for destruction (daman) in such cases, with the preferred position being no liability unless there is a specific witness scenario. A digression on selling the upper storey (uluww) and a buyer's right to rebuild after collapse is included, but the main focus returns to the principle of indication and naming (al-ishara ma'a al-tasmiya) attributed to Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani. This principle states that when a contract uses both indication and naming, if the indicated and named items differ in jins, the contract is void; if they are the same jins, the contract is valid with an option for misdescription. The passage applies this to various contracts (marriage, hire, etc.) and gives examples like buying a ruby and finding glass (different jins: void) vs. buying a red ruby and finding yellow (same jins: valid with option). An internal Hanafi dispute exists regarding whether an option exists when the actual quality is better than specified; the preferred view is that the option exists regardless, as the buyer's purpose may have been specific. Finally, the passage distinguishes between jins in logic (essential genus) and jins in fiqh (based on practical purpose). The term 'jins' in fiqh is defined as a category where the purposes do not differ greatly, and 'two jins' are categories where purposes differ greatly.

Key Terms
جنس (jins): In this passage, jins in fiqh refers to a category of items that serve similar purposes in transactions, as opposed to the logical genus (الجنس الذاتي). Two items are of different jins if the purposes for which they are acquired differ greatly.
الإشارة مع التسمية (al-ishāra ma‘a al-tasmiya): A principle of Imam Muhammad: when a contract involves both pointing to an object and naming it, if the pointed and named items are of different jins, the contract is void; if same jins, valid with khiyar for misdescription.
خيار (khiyār): Option to rescind a contract due to a defect or missing description; in this passage, khiyar al-wasf (option for misdescription).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle of Muhammad al-Shaybani, if a buyer purchases a 'slave-girl' but receives a male slave, what is the ruling?
A) The contract is valid and the buyer has an option to return the slave.
B) The contract is void because the gender difference constitutes a different jins in humans.
C) The contract is valid if the buyer accepts it.
D) The contract is suspended until the buyer inspects the slave.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school considers male and female humans as different jins in sales, so a misidentified gender voids the contract; this rests on the principle of indication and naming and the functional definition of jins.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Ruling on Sale when the Object's Genus is Misdescribed
Explains the effect of ishara vs tasmiya on genus and the option for missing attribute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between genus (jins) and attribute (wasf) in a sale, apply the Hanafi principle of ishara and tasmiya, and identify when the buyer has an option (khiyar al-wasf) due to missing attribute, including the intra-Hanafi dispute on the option when the found attribute is superior.

Core Idea
When genus mismatches, sale is void; when attribute mismatches, sale is valid but buyer has option — even if the found attribute is better.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq, the author explores the fiqh definition of genus (jins) and the effect of misdescribing the object of sale. Building on a principle from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, he explains that the combination of pointing (ishara) and naming (tasmiya) determines whether the contract is void (batil) due to genus difference or valid with an option (khiyar) due to attribute loss. You will learn the Hanafi approach to distinguishing genus from attribute and the disagreement among later Hanafi scholars about the scope of the option.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of ishara and tasmiya
When both pointing and naming are used in a sale, and they conflict, the ruling depends on whether the pointed and named objects are of the same genus. If different genera, the contract attaches to the named (which does not exist) and is void. If same genus, it attaches to the pointed object (which exists) and is valid.
This principle is foundational for determining the object of sale when description and actual object differ.
2
Define 'jins' in Hanafi fiqh
In Hanafi fiqh, genus is defined by purpose (gharad), not by essential characteristics. Objects that serve vastly different purposes are different genera, even if logically they might be one genus (e.g., male and female humans are two genera in fiqh due to different purposes).
This differs from logical genus (al-jins al-mantiqi) which is based on essential attributes.
3
Apply the principle to the examples given
Male and female humans are different genera; animals are one genus; vinegar and dates syrup are different genera; Wadhari and Zandiji dates are considered two genera despite same origin. The same principle applies to other contracts like marriage, hire, etc.
The ruling changes with whether the misdescription concerns genus or attribute.
4
Identify the option for missing attribute (khiyar al-wasf)
If the object is of the same genus but a promised attribute is missing (e.g., buying a slave described as a baker but found to be a writer), the sale is valid but the buyer has an option to confirm or rescind due to the loss of the stipulated attribute.
This option exists even if the actual attribute is considered better than the promised one, according to the preferred view.
5
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding khiyar
Some later Hanafi scholars (Sadr al-Islam, Zahir al-Din) held that the option only applies if the found attribute is inferior; others (including the author of al-Hidayah and al-Muhit) grant it regardless. The preferred view is the latter, based on loss of the buyer's purpose.
The dispute hinges on whether the buyer's purpose is deemed to be the specific attribute or any attribute of the same genus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب البيع الفاسد
يبني على الأصل الذي ذكرناه في النكاح لمحمد وهو أن الإشارة مع التسمية إذا اجتمعتا ففي مختلفي الجنس يتعلق العقد بالمسمى ويبطل لانعدامه، وفي متحدي الجنس يتعلق بالمشار إليه وينعقد لوجوده ويتخير لفوات الوصف كمن اشترى عبداً على أنه خباز فإذا هو كاتب وفي مسألتنا الذكر والأنثى من بني آدم جنسان للتفاوت في الأغراض وفي الحيوانات جنس واحد للتقارب فيها وهو المعتبر دون الأصل كالخل والدبس جنسان والوذاري والزنديجي على ما قالوا جنسان مع اتحاد أصلهما؛ كذا في الهداية. والأصل المذكور لمحمد رحمه الله تعالى متفق عليه هنا ويجري في سائر العقود من النكاح والإجارة والصلح عن دم العمد والخلع والعتق على مال والبيع في مسألة الكتاب باطل لعدم المبيع، وبه ظهر أن الذكر والأنثى من بني آدم جنسان فقهاً وإن اتحدا جنساً في المنطق لأنه الذاتي المقول على كثيرين مختلفين بمميز داخل، والجنس في الفقة المقول على كثيرين لا يتفاوت الغرض منها فاحشاً فالجنسان ما يتفاوت الغرض منهما فاحشاً بلا نظر إلى الذاتي والوذاري بفتح الواو وكسرها وإعجام الذال ثم راء مهملة نسبة إلى وذار قرية من قرى سمرقند والزنديجي بزاي ثم نون ثم دال مهملة ثم ياء ثم نسبة إلى زندنه بفتح الزاي والنون الأخيرة والجيم زيدت على خلاف القياس مع أصلهما ؛ هكذا ذكر صاحب الهداية عن المشايخ . قال في فتح القدير: ومن المختلفي الجنس ما إذا باع أنه ياقوت فإذا هو زجاج فالبيع باطل، ولو باعه ليلاً على أنه ياقوت أحمر فظهر أصفر صح ويخير كما إذا باع عبداً على أنه خباز فإذا هو كاتب ؛ هكذا ذكر المصنف وإن كانت صناعة الكتابة أشرف عند الناس من الخبز وكان المصنف ممن لا وصحح الأول لفوات غرض المشتري وكان مستند المفصلين ما تقدم فيمن اشترى عبداً على أنه كافر فإذا هو مسلم لا خيار له لأنه خير مما عين. وقد يفرق بأن الغرض وهو استخدام العبد بما لا يليق به لا يتفاوت بين مسلم وكافر من الزراعة وأمورها أو التجارة وأمورها بخلاف تعيين الخبز أو الكتابة فإنه يفيد أن حاجته التي لأجلها اشترى هي هذا الوصف اهـ. وقد ظهر من كلامهم أن من اشترى فصوصاً ثم اختلفا قال المشتري شرطت لي
