Excluding the Fetus (Istithna' al-Haml) and Its Effect on Contracts
Explains the ruling on excluding the fetus and its effect on various contracts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on excluding the fetus from the sale of a pregnant animal and distinguish which contracts are invalidated by this condition and which are not.

Core Idea
Excluding the fetus from a sale invalidates the sale in most exchange contracts but not in gratuitous contracts like gift and marriage.

The second part of the passage explains a specific invalid condition: excluding the fetus (haml) from the sale of a pregnant animal. The Hanafi jurists rule that the fetus is part of the animal, so its exclusion is an invalid condition. However, this condition does not invalidate all contracts; some contracts are robust enough to withstand invalid conditions. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the general rule
The fetus is considered like a limb of the animal; the contract of sale originally includes it. Therefore, any stipulation to exclude it is against the requirement of the contract and is an invalid condition.
The text says: 'بمنزلة أطراف الحيوان الأصل يتناوله فالاستثناء يكون على خلاف الموجب' – it is like the limbs, the contract covers it, so exception contradicts the requirement.
2
Apply to sale contract
Since the condition is invalid, and sale is a contract that is invalidated by invalid conditions (يبطل بالشروط الفاسدة), the sale becomes fasid (invalid).
The text: 'والبيع يبطل به'
3
Identify contracts invalidated
The passage lists sale (bay'), kitabah (manumission contract), ijarah (lease), and rahn (pawn) as contracts invalidated by such an invalid condition.
Kitabah has an additional nuance: the invalidator must be embedded in the core of the contract.
4
Identify contracts NOT invalidated
Contracts like hibah (gift), sadaqah (charity), nikah (marriage), khul' (divorce by redemption), sulh 'an dam al-'amd (compromise for intentional killing), and wasiyyah (will) are not invalidated. Rather, the invalid condition is disregarded, and the contract remains valid.
The text: 'لا يبطل باستثناء الحمل بل يبطل الاستثناء' – they are not invalidated by it; rather the exception is invalid.
5
Extract the underlying principle
The difference lies in the nature of these contracts. Contracts that are strictly commutative and based on exchange (sale, lease, pawn) are invalidated by invalid conditions because the condition affects the consideration. Gratuitous contracts (gift, marriage, will) are more lenient; invalid conditions are voided but the contract stands.
This is a general Hanafi principle: contracts that are not strictly commutative (tabarru'at) are not invalidated by invalid conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الشروط المفسدة ما في القنية : اشترى بطيخة على أنها حلوة أو شاة على أنها تحلب كذا أو زيتوناً أو سمسماً على أن فيه كذا مناً أو شاة أو ثوراً على أن فيه كذا مناً من اللحم فسد البيع في الكل لتعذر معرفته قبل العمل وعجز البائع عن الوفاء به اهـ.
إذا دبره لاتصاله خلقة به وبيع يصح إفراده ولو اشتراه على أن يؤدي الثمن من بيعه فهو فاسد إن شرط، وإنما ذكر استثناء الحمل من الشروط لأنه لما كان غير صحيح صار شرطاً فاسداً، والأصل فيه أن ما لا بالعقد لا يصح استثناؤه من العقد والحمل من هذا القبيل، وهذا لأنه بمنزلة أطراف الحيوان الأصل يتناوله فالاستثناء يكون على خلاف الموجب فلم يصح فيصير شرطاً فاسداً والبيع يبطل به والكتابة والإجارة والرهن بمنزلة البيع لأنها تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة غير أن المفسد في الكتابة ما يتمكن في صلب العقد منها والهبة والصدقة والنكاح والخلع والصلح عن دم العمد لا يبطل باستثناء الحمل بل يبطل الاستثناء لأن هذه العقود لا تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة، وكذلك الوصية لا تبطل به لكن . الاستثناء حتى يكون الحمل يصح والشركة» قوله : (فإن المشتري إذا أعتقه أي بعد القبض كما في النهر ثم قال : وأجمعوا على أنه لو أعتقه قبل القبض لا يجوز قوله : والبيع يبطل (به قال الرملي مراده يفسد وقد تبعه في النهر في

Condition vs Promise in Sale: The Building Walls Example
Ruling on condition vs promise in sale contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a condition (shart) and a promise (wa'd) in a sale contract, and state the legal effect of each according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A condition not harmonious with the contract invalidates the sale; a promise does not.

When a seller adds a stipulation to a sale that is not normally part of the transaction—like building walls for the buyer—the legal classification of that stipulation determines whether the contract remains valid. The Hanafi jurists carefully distinguish between a condition (shart) and a promise (wa'd). This lesson unpacks that distinction using the example of building walls and the famous hadith of Barira.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the stipulation
What is the sale? What additional clause is attached? In our example, a garden is sold with the seller undertaking to build its walls.
The stipulation is not naturally part of a sale of gardens.
2
Classify the clause as condition or promise
Examine the wording. Is it expressed as a condition (e.g., 'on condition that') or as a promise (e.g., 'until I build')? The wording determines its legal nature.
The text cites Ishaq al-Walwaliji: 'Buy until I build the walls' indicates a promise.
3
Apply the ruling
If the clause is a condition (shart) that is not harmonious with the contract, the sale becomes void (fasid). If it is a promise (wa'd), the sale remains valid (sahih), but the buyer retains the option to rescind (khiyar) if the seller does not fulfill the promise.
A void sale must be remade; a valid sale with khiyar gives the buyer a choice.
4
Examine the evidence from hadith
The hadith of Barira is cited: the sale of Barira was valid because there was only a promise to free her, not a condition. This shows that a promise does not affect the validity of the sale.
This is a dalil naqli (textual proof) for the rule.
5
Understand the school's reasoning
Conditions that oppose the natural demands of the contract (ghayr mula'im) cause fasad because they conflict with the contract's purpose. Promises are not conditions; they are independent undertakings that do not alter the contract's essential nature.
The distinction is based on the intention and wording of the parties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقتضه العقد لا يرجع وهو فاسداً فلا الشرط لأنه لو أخرجه مخرج الوعد لم يفسد كما إذا باع بستاناً على أن يعمر حوائطه وأخرجه مخرج الوعد ولكن لو لم يبن البائع لم يجبر ويخير المشتري في الرد ; كذا في الذخيرة لكن لم يبين بماذا يكون إخراجه مخرج الوعد وهو أحد الأجوبة عن حديث بريره فإن البيع لم يكن بشرط العتق وإنما كان بوعد عتقها وبين الإمام اسحق الولوالجي صورة إخراجه مخرج الوعد قال : اشتر حتى ابني الحوائط
The contract requires it, it does not return, and it is void, so the condition [invalidates] because if he brings it out as a promise, it does not invalidate, as when he sold a garden on condition that he builds its walls and brought it out as a promise, but if the seller did not build, he is not compelled and the buyer has the option to return. Thus in al-Dhakhira, but it did not clarify how to bring it out as a promise, and it is one of the answers to the hadith of Barira, for the sale was not with a condition of freeing but rather with a promise of freeing her. And Imam Ishaq al-Walwaliji explained the form of bringing it out as a promise: he said: 'Buy until I build the walls.'
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on a condition in a sale contract that is not in harmony with the contract (ghayr mula'im). The author distinguishes between a condition (shart) and a promise (wa'd). If the clause is expressed as a condition, the sale becomes void (fasid). If expressed as a promise, the sale remains valid. The example given is selling a garden with the condition that the seller builds its walls. If phrased as a condition, the sale is void. If phrased as a promise, the sale is valid, but if the seller fails to build, the buyer has the option to rescind (khiyar). The text then references the hadith of Barira, where the sale of a slave woman was not with a condition of freeing her but with a promise to free her. Ishaq al-Walwaliji clarifies the form: the seller says 'Buy until I build the walls' – this indicates a promise, not a condition. This is an important distinction: a condition that is not in harmony with the contract invalidates it, but a promise does not.

Key Terms
شرط (shart): A stipulation in a contract that is not harmonious (ghayr mula'im) invalidates the sale if introduced as a condition.
وعد (wa'd): A promise; does not affect the validity of the contract.
ملائم (mula'im): Harmonious with the contract; conditions that are mula'im are valid, others cause fasad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the legal effect of a condition that is not harmonious with the contract (ghayr mula'im) in a sale according to the Hanafi school?
A) The sale is valid but the buyer has an option to rescind
B) The sale is void (fasid)
C) The condition is ignored and the sale stands
D) The sale is suspended until the condition is fulfilled

Key Takeaway
The wording of a stipulation determines its legal effect: a condition that is not harmonious invalidates the sale, while a promise does not affect validity but grants the buyer an option.

Conditions of Intercourse in Sale: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on condition of intercourse or abstention in sale of slave-woman

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani on conditions of intercourse in the sale of a slave-woman and apply the concept of mula'im to evaluate similar conditions.

Core Idea
A condition that restricts the buyer's absolute right voids the sale unless it is harmonious or benefits no one.

The Hanafi school generally holds that a sale must grant the buyer unrestricted ownership. However, when a condition specifying intercourse or abstention is attached to the sale of a slave-woman, the three founding imams disagree. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position and shows how the principle of 'harmony' (mula'im) determines validity.

Guided Path
1
State the default Hanafi position
Begin with Abu Hanifa's view: any condition that qualifies the buyer's right to use his property (like 'must have intercourse' or 'must not') is not harmonious with the contract's purpose (itlaq). Therefore the sale is void (fasid).
This is the baseline; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad offer exceptions.
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's partial exception
Abu Yusuf allows the condition of having intercourse because it is a natural benefit of ownership and thus harmonious (mula'im). He does not allow the condition of abstaining.
He focuses on benefit (manfa'ah) aligning with the contract.
3
Identify Muhammad's broader allowance
Muhammad allows both conditions. For intercourse, he agrees with Abu Yusuf. For abstention, if the condition benefits no one, he considers it 'a condition with no claimant' (shart la talib lahu) and thus harmless.
Muhammad introduces a new category: conditions that do not affect any party's interest.
4
Trace the reasoning: itlaq vs mula'im vs shart la talib lahu
Abu Hanifa emphasizes absolute freedom (itlaq). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow deviations if the condition is harmonious (mula'im) or neutral. Understand how each imam weighs the purpose of the contract.
The dispute is about interpreting 'harmonious condition'.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, such a sale is void and must be redone without the condition. Under Abu Yusuf, the sale with a condition of intercourse is valid. Under Muhammad, both conditions are valid. This affects the binding nature of the contract.
A practicing Hanafi should follow the dominant view unless a mufti chooses otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخرج عن الملائم للعقد ما لو اشترى أمة بشرط أن يطأها المشتري أو لا يطأها فالبيع فاسد لأن الملائم للعقد الإطلاق. وعند أبي يوسف يجوز في الأول لأنه ملائم، وعند محمد يجوز فيهما في الأول لما قاله أبو يوسف، وفي الثاني إن لم نفعه إلى أحد فهو شرط لا طالب له
And excluded from harmonious to the contract is if he bought a slave-woman on condition that the buyer has intercourse with her or does not have intercourse, then the sale is void because what is harmonious to the contract is absolute freedom. According to Abu Yusuf, it is permissible in the first [condition of intercourse] because it is harmonious. According to Muhammad, it is permissible in both – in the first for what Abu Yusuf said, and in the second if it does not benefit anyone then it is a condition with no claimant.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding conditions of intercourse in the sale of a slave-woman. The default position (Abu Hanifa) is that any condition specifying intercourse or abstention is not harmonious with the contract, because the contract requires absolute freedom (itlaq) – i.e., the buyer should have unrestricted rights. Therefore, such conditions render the sale void (fasid). Abu Yusuf differs: he considers the condition of having intercourse (first case) as harmonious, because intercourse is a natural benefit of ownership, and thus allows it. Muhammad goes further: he allows both conditions. In the case of abstaining (second case), if the condition does not benefit anyone (la naf' fihi li-ahad), he considers it a condition with no claimant, so it does not invalidate the sale. This shows the nuance in Hanafi jurisprudence: conditions are evaluated based on their harmony with the contract and whether they have a tangible benefit.

Key Terms
ملائم (mula'im): Harmonious with the purpose of the contract; conditions that are mula'im are valid.
إطلاق (itlaq): Absolute freedom; the contract of sale inherently requires unrestricted ownership for the buyer.
شرط لا طالب له (shart la talib lahu): A condition that benefits no one; considered harmless by Muhammad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, when is the condition of abstaining from intercourse permissible in a slave-woman sale?
A) When it benefits the seller
B) When it benefits the buyer
C) When it benefits no one
D) It is never permissible

Key Takeaway
A condition that limits the buyer's absolute ownership is generally invalid, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow some exceptions based on harmony or lack of benefit.

Sale with Condition of Freeing: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sale with condition of freeing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain Abu Hanifa's reasoning that a void sale can become valid upon actual freeing, and distinguish the Sahibayn's position along with the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Freeing a slave after a void sale can retroactively validate it because it harmonizes with ownership termination.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a sale contract that includes a condition for the buyer to free the slave. The condition itself makes the sale void (fasid) initially. However, if the buyer actually frees the slave after purchase, do we retroactively validate the sale? Abu Hanifa says yes, while his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say no. Understanding the reasoning behind each position and the practical difference is essential for mastering Hanafi contract law.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What is Abu Hanifa's ruling when the buyer actually frees the slave?
He rules the sale becomes valid and the agreed price is due.
2
Identify the Sahibayn's objection
How do Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule, and what is their basic reasoning?
They rule the sale remains void and the market value (qimah) is due, because the sale was void from the start.
3
Follow the proof of the Sahibayn
The Sahibayn draw an analogy (qiyas) to other forms of destruction or disposal of the slave. What happens if the buyer destroys the slave?
If the buyer destroys, sells, or gifts the slave, all agree the value is due. The Sahibayn say freeing is similar.
4
Analyse Abu Hanifa's response
How does Abu Hanifa distinguish freeing from other acts? What concept does he use?
He introduces the concept of 'harmony in consequence' (mula'imah bi al-hukm): freeing terminates ownership, thus harmonizes with the contract's purpose, unlike birth or becoming umm walad.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference between the two positions?
Under Abu Hanifa, the seller receives the agreed price; under Sahibayn, the seller receives the market value (which may differ).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يفصل المؤلف بين شرط الفساد وشرط في كذلك إلا البيع بشرط العتق فإن المشتري إذا أعتقه البيع ووجب صح الثمن عليه عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : يبقى فاسداً فتجب القيمة لأن البيع قد وقع ينقلب جائزاً كما إذا تلف بوجه آخر . ولأبي حنيفة أن شرط العتق من حيث ذاته لا يلائم العقد على ما ذكرناه ولكن من حيث حكمه يلائمه لأنه منتهي للملك والشيء بانتهائه يتقرر، ولهذا لا يمنع العتق الرجوع بنقصان العيب فإذا أتلف بوجه آخر لا تتحقق الملائمة فتقرر الفساد، وإذا وجد العتق تحققت الملائمة فترجح جانب الجواز فكان الحال موقوفاً بخلاف ما ها أو استولدها فإنهما لا ينهيان الملك الجواز قضاء قاض ببيعهما، وأجمعوا أن المشتري لو أتلفه أو باعه أو وهبه تلزمه قيمته ; كذا في السراج الوهاج
And the author did not distinguish between a condition of invalidity and a condition in such a way except for the sale with a condition of freeing: for if the buyer frees him, the sale [becomes valid] and the price is due according to Abu Hanifa. And the two said: it remains void and the value is due because the sale has occurred, it becomes valid as if it perished in another way. And for Abu Hanifa, the condition of freeing in its essence does not harmonize with the contract as we mentioned, but in its ruling it harmonizes because it terminates ownership and the thing by its termination is established, and for this reason freeing does not prevent claiming a reduction for defect; so if it perishes in another way, harmony is not realized and voidness is established, and if freeing occurs, harmony is realized and the side of validity prevails, so the situation is suspended, unlike if he [the buyer] caused her to give birth or she became umm walad, for they do not terminate ownership with validity of a judge's sale of them. And they agreed that if the buyer destroys it or sells it or gives it as a gift, its value is due. Thus in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage details the dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding a sale made with the condition that the buyer frees the slave. Initially, such a sale is void (fasid) because the condition is not harmonious. However, if the buyer actually frees the slave after purchase, Abu Hanifa rules that the sale becomes valid (the price is due). The Sahibayn argue that the sale remains void and only the slave's value is due. Abu Hanifa's reasoning: the condition of freeing is inherently not harmonious, but its consequence (ending ownership) is harmonious because it finalizes the transaction. Thus, if the freeing occurs, harmony is realized and the sale becomes valid. He compares it to the case where the slave perishes in another way – then voidness is established. But if the buyer causes the slave to give birth or she becomes umm walad (mother of a child), these do not make the sale valid because they do not terminate ownership in a way that a judge can sell them. Additionally, all agree that if the buyer destroys, sells, or gives away the slave, the value must be paid. This illustrates the Hanafi principle that a void sale can become valid if an act harmonizes with the contract's purpose.

Key Terms
عتق ('itq): Freeing; a condition of freeing in a sale contract causes initial voidness, but actual freeing may validate the sale according to Abu Hanifa.
قيمة (qimah): Value; the market value of the slave that must be paid if the sale remains void.
ملائمة بالحكم (mula'imah bi al-hukm): Harmonious in consequence; used by Abu Hanifa to argue that freeing ultimately aligns with the contract's purpose.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling of Abu Hanifa on a sale with a condition of freeing if the buyer actually frees the slave?
A) The sale becomes valid and the price is due
B) The sale remains void and the value is due
C) The sale becomes void and the slave is returned
D) The condition is ignored and the sale is valid from the start

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's concept of harmony in consequence allows a void sale to become valid upon actual freeing, altering the seller's entitlement from market value to agreed price.

Void Conditions in Sale from al-Qunya
Listing conditions that corrupt a sale due to impossibility or uncertainty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify corrupting conditions (shurut mufsidah) in a sale contract and explain why they render the contract void (fasid) according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A sale condition that cannot be verified before performance corrupts the contract due to uncertainty.

This passage from al-Qunya lists several examples of conditions that, when included in a sale, render the contract void (fasid). The common thread is that these conditions involve qualities or quantities that cannot be known at the time of contract and that the seller cannot guarantee. Understanding these examples helps you apply the Hanafi principle forbidding uncertainty (gharar) in sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition
Look at each condition listed: sweetness of watermelon, milk yield of sheep, weight of olives/sesame, weight of meat.
2
Recognize the uncertainty
Explain why each condition is unknown at the time of sale and why the seller cannot fulfill it (e.g., sweetness unknown before cutting).
3
Apply the ruling
State that the sale is fasid (void) because of the condition's uncertainty and the seller's inability to guarantee it.
4
Generalize the principle
Derive the broader rule: any condition involving an unverifiable quality or impossible guarantee corrupts the sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الشروط المفسدة ما في القنية : اشترى بطيخة على أنها حلوة أو شاة على أنها تحلب كذا أو زيتوناً أو سمسماً على أن فيه كذا مناً أو شاة أو ثوراً على أن فيه كذا مناً من اللحم فسد البيع في الكل لتعذر معرفته قبل العمل وعجز البائع عن الوفاء به اهـ.
And among the corrupting conditions is what is in al-Qunya: if he bought a watermelon on condition that it is sweet, or a sheep on condition that it gives such-and-such amount of milk, or olives or sesame on condition that it contains such-and-such mann, or a sheep or bull on condition that it contains such-and-such mann of meat, the sale is void in all cases because knowledge of it before the act is difficult and the seller is unable to fulfill it.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage lists several conditions that corrupt a sale (make it fasid) according to the Hanafi school, as recorded in al-Qunya. These conditions involve unknown or unascertainable qualities: sweetness of a watermelon, milk yield of a sheep, weight of olives or sesame, amount of meat from an animal. The reasoning is that it is impossible to know these before the act (e.g., tasting, milking, slaughtering) and the seller cannot guarantee the specified quantity or quality. Such conditions render the sale void because they create uncertainty (gharar) and inability to perform. This highlights the Hanafi principle that conditions involving unknown future events or impossible guarantees invalidate the contract.

Key Terms
شروط مفسدة (shurut mufsidah): Corrupting conditions; conditions that render the sale void (fasid) due to uncertainty or impossibility.
تعذر المعرفة (ta'adhdhur al-ma'rifah): Difficulty of knowing; used as a reason for fasad when the condition cannot be verified.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because watermelon is not sold by weight
D) Because the condition is not mentioned in the contract

Key Takeaway
Conditions that cannot be verified or guaranteed at the time of sale corrupt the contract and render it void (fasid) in the Hanafi school.

Exception of the Fetus: Void Condition in Sale Contracts
Ruling on excepting the fetus from a sale and its effect on various contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between contracts that are invalidated by the void condition of excepting the fetus and those that remain valid, and explain the rationale (talil) behind the Hanafi rule.

Core Idea
Excepting the fetus in a sale invalidates because it contradicts inherent inclusion; marriage, gift, and will are not invalidated—only the condition is void.

This passage from Baḥr al-Rāʾiq explains a critical principle: not every void condition affects a contract. Some contracts, like sale, are strict—any condition contrary to the nature of the contract voids it. Others, like marriage and gift, are lenient—the condition is void but the contract stands. The exception (istithnā) of the fetus serves as the test case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (excepting the fetus from a sale) and the legal outcome (the sale is fasid – void).
The act is a condition (shart) in the contract.
2
Locate the dalil (talil)
Find the reasoning provided: the fetus is like a limb, so the contract inherently includes it; exception is contrary to the requirement (khilāf al-mūjib).
This is a causal proof based on the nature of the subject matter.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalālah
Explain why this reasoning proves the ruling: the condition is void, and sale is a contract that is invalidated by void conditions.
The rule for sale: al-bayʿ yabṭul bi al-shurūṭ al-fāsidah.
4
Identify the exception
List the contracts that are NOT invalidated by this condition: hibah, sadaqah, nikah, khulʿ, ṣulḥ over intentional homicide, and wasiyyah.
These contracts are lenient: they are not invalidated by void conditions.
5
State the thamarah al-khilāf
The practical difference: in sale, the contract is void and must be redone; in these other contracts, only the condition is void and the contract remains valid.
This affects the rights and obligations of the parties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا دبره لاتصاله خلقة به وبيع يصح إفراده. ولو اشتراه على أن يؤدي الثمن من بيعه فهو فاسد إن شرط، وإنما ذكر استثناء الحمل من الشروط لأنه لما كان غير صحيح صار شرطاً فاسداً، والأصل فيه أن ما لا بالعقد لا يصح استثناؤه من العقد والحمل من هذا القبيل، وهذا لأنه بمنزلة أطراف الحيوان الأصل يتناوله فالاستثناء يكون على خلاف الموجب فلم يصح فيصير شرطاً فاسداً والبيع يبطل به والكتابة والإجارة والرهن بمنزلة البيع لأنها تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة غير أن المفسد في الكتابة ما يتمكن في صلب العقد منها والهبة والصدقة والنكاح والخلع والصلح عن دم العمد لا يبطل باستثناء الحمل بل يبطل الاستثناء لأن هذه العقود لا تبطل بالشروط الفاسدة، وكذلك الوصية لا تبطل به لكن . الاستثناء حتى يكون الحمل يصح والشركة»
If he has a fetus? due to its connection by nature? and sale [of what?] is valid to separate it. And if he bought it on condition that he pays the price from its sale, it is void if conditioned. And the exception of the fetus is mentioned among conditions because since it is invalid, it becomes a void condition. The principle is that what is not valid in [exception from] the contract is not valid to be excepted from the contract, and the fetus is of this type. This is because it is like the limbs of the animal: the contract includes it, so the exception is contrary to the requirement, so it is not valid and becomes a void condition, and the sale is invalidated by it. And kitabah, ijarah, and rahn are like sale because they are invalidated by void conditions, except that the invalidating factor in kitabah is what is established in the contract itself. And hibah, sadaqah, nikah, khul', and sulh over intentional homicide are not invalidated by excepting the fetus; rather, the exception is invalidated, because these contracts are not invalidated by void conditions. Similarly, wasiyyah is not invalidated by it, but the exception [remains] so that the fetus is valid and [?] الشركة
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage discusses the exception (istithna) of the fetus from a sale contract. The author states that excepting the fetus is not valid because the fetus is part of the animal (like its limbs) and the contract of sale automatically includes it. Therefore, attempting to exclude it is a condition contrary to the requirement (khilaf al-mujib), making the sale void (fasid). This applies to sale, kitabah (contract of manumission), ijarah (hire), and rahn (pledge) – all of which are invalidated by void conditions. However, for other contracts like gift (hibah), charity (sadaqah), marriage (nikah), khul', and sulh over blood money, the contract itself is not invalidated by a void condition; rather, the condition is invalid and the contract remains valid without the exception. Similarly, a will (wasiyyah) is not invalidated, and the fetus can be validly excepted in a will? The text also mentions that in the case of kitabah, only conditions that are part of the contract's core invalidate, not peripheral ones. This shows a key difference: some contracts, like marriage and gift, are not easily invalidated by conditions, unlike sale contracts where conditions are strict.

Key Terms
استثناء الحمل (istithna al-haml): Excepting the fetus from a sale; invalidates the sale because the fetus is inherently included.
عقد لا يبطل بالشروط الفاسدة ('aqd la yabtul bi al-shurut al-fasidah): Contracts that are not invalidated by void conditions, such as marriage, gift, will.
خلاف الموجب (khilaf al-mujib): Contrary to the requirement of the contract; when a condition contradicts what the contract inherently demands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which contracts are NOT invalidated by a void condition of excepting the fetus?
A) Sale, hire, pledge
B) Marriage, gift, will
C) Kitabah, sale, hire
D) All contracts are invalidated

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school strictly distinguishes between contracts that are invalidated by void conditions (sale, hire, pledge, kitabah) and those that are not (marriage, gift, will, etc.), with the exception of the fetus illustrating this principle.

Conditions for Valid Exception (Istithna) in Sales
Opening statement of the istithna rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for a valid exception in a sale and apply it to scenarios involving separable and non-separable items.

Core Idea
An exception is valid only if the excepted item can be individually separated and specified – otherwise it is void.

This lesson unpacks the foundational Hanafi rule for exceptions in sales. The key is whether the excepted item can be physically or legally separated from the whole. We will examine the classic examples of a pile (sabra) and a flock of sheep to internalize this principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is 'making an exception in a sale' – excluding an item from the sale. The ruling depends on the nature of the item.
2
Distinguish separable vs. non-separable
A pile (sabra) is separable because each grain can be separated. A flock of sheep is mixed and cannot be separated unless a specific sheep is pointed out.
The term 'sabra' refers to a heap of grain or similar.
3
Apply to the sheep exception
If the sheep is unspecified ('a sheep'), the exception is invalid due to gharar. If specified by pointing ('this sheep'), it becomes separable and the exception is valid.
4
Generalize the rule
The same applies to any countable items that vary in value (mutafawit): they must be individually specified for a valid exception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتفرع على القاعدة أنه يصح الصبرة لجواز إفراده ولا يصح استثناء شاة من قطيع لعدم جواز إفرادها من قطيع إذا لم تكن معينة، وأما إذا عينها بالإشارة فالاستثناء صحيح، وكذا الحال في كل عددي متفاوت. استثناء أرطال معلومة من بيع الثمرة الجواز إيراده على الأرطال ابتداء وهو ومن مسائل الاستثناء : باع صبرة بمائة إلا عشرها فله تسعة أعشارها بجميع الثمن، ولو قال على أن عشرها لي فله تسعة أعشارها بتسعة أعشار الثمن خلافاً للمروي عن محمد أنه بالجميع. وعن أبي يوسف أنه لو قال أبيعك هذه المائة شاة بمائة على أن هذه لي أو ولي هذه فسد، ولو قال إلا هذه كان ما بقي بمائة ولو قال ولي نصفها كان النصف بخمسين، ولو قال بعتك هذا العبد بألف إلا نصفه بخمسمائة عن محمد جاز في كله بألف وخمسمائة لأن المعنى باع نصفه بألف لأنه الباقي بعد الاستثناء فالنصف المستثنى عين بيعه بخمسمائة. ولو قال على أن لي نصفه بثلاثمائة أو مائة دينار فسد لإدخال صفقة في صفقة. ولو قال بعتك الدار الخارجة على أن تجعل لي طريقاً إلى داري هذه الداخلة فسد البيع، ولو قال إلا طريقاً إلى داري الداخلة جاز وطريقه عرض باب الدار الخارجة. ولو باع بيتاً على أن لا طريق للمشتري في الدار وعلى أن بابه في الدهليز يجوز ، ولو زعم أن له طريقاً فظهر أن لا طريق له يرد، ولو باع بألف دينار إلا درهماً أو إلا ثوباً أو إلا كر حنطة أو هذه الشياه إلا واحدة لا يجوز، ولو كانت بعينها جاز، ولو باع داراً على أن لا بناء فيها فإذا فيها بناء فالبيع فاسد لأنه يحتاج إلى نقض البناء، ولو باعها على أن بناءها من آجر فإذا هو لين فسد بناء على أنهما جنسان كما لو باعه ثوباً على أنه هروي فظهر بلخياً. ولو باع الأرض على أن فيها بناء فإذا لا بناء فيها أو اشتراها بشجرها فليس فيها شجر جاز وله الخيار وكذا لو باع بعلوها وسفلها فظهر أن لا علو لها ومثله لو اشترى بأجذاعها؛ كذا في فتح القدير .
And branching from the rule: it is valid to except a pile (sabra) because it can be separated individually, and it is not valid to except a sheep from a flock if not specified; but if specified by pointing, the exception is valid. Similarly for every varying countable. Exception of known ratls from the sale of fruit – the permissibility to present it on ratls initially? And from the issues of exception: if he sold a pile for one hundred except its tenth, he gets nine-tenths for the whole price; if he said 'on condition that its tenth is mine', he gets nine-tenths for nine-tenths of the price, contrary to what is narrated from Muhammad that it is for the whole. And from Abu Yusuf: if he said 'I sell you these one hundred sheep for one hundred on condition that this one is mine' or 'that I have this one', it is void; if he said 'except this one', the remaining is for one hundred; if he said 'and I have half of it', the half is for fifty; if he said 'I sold you this slave for one thousand except half for five hundred', according to Muhammad it is valid for the whole for one thousand five hundred, because the meaning is that he sold half for one thousand [since it is the remaining after exception] and the excepted half is a separate sale for five hundred. If he said 'on condition that I have half for three hundred or one hundred dinars', it is void for including one contract in another. If he said 'I sold you the outer house on condition that you make a path to this inner house of mine', the sale is void; if he said 'except a path to my inner house', it is valid and his path is the width of the door of the outer house. If he sold a house on condition that the buyer has no path in the courtyard and that his door is in the hallway, it is valid; if he claimed he has a path and it turns out he has no path, he returns. If he sold for one thousand dinars except one dirham, or except one garment, or except one kurr of wheat, or except one of these sheep without specification, it is not valid; if it is specified individually, it is valid. If he sold a house on condition that there is no building in it, and there is a building, the sale is void because it requires demolition. If he sold it on condition that its building is of brick, and it is of mud, it is void based on that they are two types, as if he sold a garment on condition it is Herawi and it appears to be Balkhi. If he sold land on condition that there is a building on it, and there is no building, or he bought it with its trees and there are no trees, it is valid and he has the option. Similarly, if he sold it with its upper and lower parts and it turns out there is no upper part, and similarly if he bought it with its palm trunks; thus in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive series of rulings on exceptions (istithna) in sales. The key principle: an exception is valid only if the excepted item can be individually separated from the whole. For example, excepting a pile (sabra) is valid because it can be separated; excepting an unspecified sheep from a flock is not valid because the sheep are mixed. If the sheep is specified by pointing, the exception is valid. The text then gives numerous examples: (1) Selling a pile for 100 except its tenth: if expressed as 'except its tenth', the seller gets nine-tenths of the pile for the full price; if expressed as 'on condition that its tenth is mine', he gets nine-tenths for nine-tenths of the price. (2) Selling 100 sheep for 100 with an exception of one specific sheep: if expressed as 'on condition that this sheep is mine', it is void; if 'except this one', valid with the remaining for 100. (3) Selling a slave for 1000 except half: Muhammad says it is valid for 1500, considering two sales. (4) Selling a house with condition of a path: if conditioned, void; if excepted, valid. (5) Various rulings on conditions about building material, existence of building, etc., where the buyer gets an option (khiyar) if the condition is not met. This shows the Hanafi approach to contracts: precision in wording is crucial to determine validity.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna): Exception; excluding a specific item from the sale. Valid only if the item can be specified and separated.
صبرة (sabra): A pile or heap, typically of grain, whose particles are separable.
متفرق (mutafarriq): Separable; the quality of a sold item whose parts can be individually taken.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is excepting a sheep from a flock valid in a sale?
A) When the sheep is specified by pointing
B) Always valid
C) When the flock is large
D) When the buyer agrees

Key Takeaway
A valid exception in a sale requires that the excepted item be both separable from the whole and specifically identified.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Pricing with Exceptions: Fractions and Multiple Items
Rulings on fractional exceptions and multi-item sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rulings for sales involving exceptions of fractions (e.g., 1/10, half) and determine the price due based on the wording.

Core Idea
The price for the remaining sold item depends on how the exception is phrased – 'except' vs. 'on condition that mine' – leading to different valuations.

This lesson delves into more complex examples of istithna where the exception involves a fractional share of a pile or multiple items (sheep, slave). The wording of the exception crucially affects whether the seller receives the full price or a proportional price for the remaining portion.

Guided Path
1
Parse the wording of the exception
Identify whether the phrase is 'except its tenth' or 'on condition that its tenth is mine'. This determines the legal effect.
Also note the difference between 'except this one' and 'this one is mine'.
2
Apply the rule for 'except'
If the seller says 'except its tenth', the sale is for the remaining nine-tenths for the full price (the excepted portion is not paid for).
The buyer gets nine-tenths of the pile but pays the full price because the exception is a deduction from the price? Actually, the text says 'he gets nine-tenths for the whole price' – meaning the seller receives the full price for the remaining.
3
Apply the rule for 'on condition that it is mine'
If the seller says 'on condition that its tenth is mine', the price is reduced proportionally: the buyer pays nine-tenths of the price for nine-tenths of the pile.
This is because the seller is retaining ownership of a share, so the buyer only pays for the share he receives.
4
Understand the disagreement with Muhammad
There is a narration from Muhammad that even with 'on condition that mine', the full price is due. The relied-upon view (in the Hidayah) is the proportional view.
This shows an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
5
Apply to the sheep and slave examples
For 100 sheep sold for 100: if the seller says 'on condition that this sheep is mine', the sale is void because it combines an exception with a condition of retention; if 'except this sheep', the remaining 99 sheep are for 100 (the seller gets the full 100). If the seller says 'and I have half of it', half is for 50. For the slave sold for 1000 except half for 500: according to Muhammad, it is valid for 1500, treating as two separate sales.
This highlights the principle of merging contracts (صفقة في صفقة) – when the wording creates two distinct transactions, it may be valid if each is independent.

Key Terms
صفقة في صفقة (safqah fi safqah): Merging two contracts into one; prohibited in Hanafi law.
استثناء (istithna): Exception; excluding a specific item from the sale.
ثمن (thaman): Price; the consideration in a sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a seller says, 'I sell you this pile of wheat for 100 dirhams except its tenth', what is the price for the nine-tenths?
A) 90 dirhams
B) 100 dirhams
C) 90 dirhams for nine-tenths and the seller keeps the tenth
D) The sale is void

Key Takeaway
In fractional exceptions, the phrase 'except' means the seller receives full price for the remainder; 'on condition that it is mine' means the price is proportional to what the buyer receives.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Conditions and Options in Sales with Exceptions
Conditions and khiyar in istithna sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid conditions attached to a sale involving exceptions and identify when the buyer gets an option (khiyar) due to misrepresentation.

Core Idea
A condition in a sale is valid if it does not create uncertainty; if a condition is false, the buyer may have the option to rescind or affirm.

The final part of the passage deals with conditions that accompany a sale, such as the presence of a building, material quality, or right of way. The Hanafi school distinguishes between conditions that are permissible and those that void the sale, and specifies when the buyer gets an option if the condition is not met.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition attached to the sale
Look for phrases like 'on condition that there is no building' or 'with the condition that it is of brick'. Determine if the condition relates to the existence or quality of a feature.
2
Check if the condition is lawful (ja'iz)
Some conditions are valid: e.g., 'on condition that there is no path' (restrictive) or 'on condition that the building is of brick' (specifying type).
Conditions that are part of the subject matter are generally valid.
3
Determine if the condition is false
If the condition is not met (e.g., a building exists when it was said to be absent, or the building is mud rather than brick), the seller has misrepresented.
4
Identify the consequence: fasid vs. khiyar
If the condition concerns a fundamental attribute that affects the contract's nature, the sale is void (fasid). If it is a subsidiary attribute, the buyer gets the option (khiyar) to rescind or affirm.
The text provides examples: selling a house on condition there is no building – if building exists, sale is fasid (needs demolition). Selling land on condition there is a building – if no building, sale is valid with khiyar.
5
Apply to the path examples
Selling a house on condition the buyer has no path is valid. If the buyer later claims a path and it's false, he returns the house.
The condition about path is a right of way; it can be waived.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتفرع على القاعدة أنه يصح الصبرة لجواز إفراده ولا يصح استثناء شاة من قطيع لعدم جواز إفرادها من قطيع إذا لم تكن معينة، وأما إذا عينها بالإشارة فالاستثناء صحيح، وكذا الحال في كل عددي متفاوت. استثناء أرطال معلومة من بيع الثمرة الجواز إيراده على الأرطال ابتداء وهو ومن مسائل الاستثناء : باع صبرة بمائة إلا عشرها فله تسعة أعشارها بجميع الثمن، ولو قال على أن عشرها لي فله تسعة أعشارها بتسعة أعشار الثمن خلافاً للمروي عن محمد أنه بالجميع. وعن أبي يوسف أنه لو قال أبيعك هذه المائة شاة بمائة على أن هذه لي أو ولي هذه فسد، ولو قال إلا هذه كان ما بقي بمائة ولو قال ولي نصفها كان النصف بخمسين، ولو قال بعتك هذا العبد بألف إلا نصفه بخمسمائة عن محمد جاز في كله بألف وخمسمائة لأن المعنى باع نصفه بألف لأنه الباقي بعد الاستثناء فالنصف المستثنى عين بيعه بخمسمائة. ولو قال على أن لي نصفه بثلاثمائة أو مائة دينار فسد لإدخال صفقة في صفقة. ولو قال بعتك الدار الخارجة على أن تجعل لي طريقاً إلى داري هذه الداخلة فسد البيع، ولو قال إلا طريقاً إلى داري الداخلة جاز وطريقه عرض باب الدار الخارجة. ولو باع بيتاً على أن لا طريق للمشتري في الدار وعلى أن بابه في الدهليز يجوز ، ولو زعم أن له طريقاً فظهر أن لا طريق له يرد، ولو باع بألف دينار إلا درهماً أو إلا ثوباً أو إلا كر حنطة أو هذه الشياه إلا واحدة لا يجوز، ولو كانت بعينها جاز، ولو باع داراً على أن لا بناء فيها فإذا فيها بناء فالبيع فاسد لأنه يحتاج إلى نقض البناء، ولو باعها على أن بناءها من آجر فإذا هو لين فسد بناء على أنهما جنسان كما لو باعه ثوباً على أنه هروي فظهر بلخياً. ولو باع الأرض على أن فيها بناء فإذا لا بناء فيها أو اشتراها بشجرها فليس فيها شجر جاز وله الخيار وكذا لو باع بعلوها وسفلها فظهر أن لا علو لها ومثله لو اشترى بأجذاعها؛ كذا في فتح القدير .

(2) Selling 100 sheep for 100 with an exception of one specific sheep: if expressed as 'on condition that this sheep is mine', it is void; if 'except this one', valid with the remaining for 100. (3) Selling a slave for 1000 except half: Muhammad says it is valid for 1500, considering two sales. (4) Selling a house with condition of a path: if conditioned, void; if excepted, valid. (5) Various rulings on conditions about building material, existence of building, etc., where the buyer gets an option (khiyar) if the condition is not met. This shows the Hanafi approach to contracts: precision in wording is crucial to determine validity.

Key Terms
خيار (khiyar): Option; the buyer's right to rescind a sale if a condition is not met or a defect exists.
جنس (jins): Type, genus; items of different jins are considered fundamentally different, causing fasād if misrepresented.
طريق (tariq): Path or right of way; a condition about path rights can be valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If land is sold 'on condition that it has a building' and there is no building, the sale is:
A) Void
B) Valid and the buyer has the option to rescind
C) Valid without any option
D) Fasid because the condition is impossible

Key Takeaway
False conditions in a sale may render the contract void (if about type) or give the buyer an option (if about existence); correct classification is crucial for the ruling.

Sale with Condition of Making and Sharing Profit: Istihsan Over Qiyas
Ruling on sale with condition of making and sharing profit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school validates a sale with a condition of making and sharing profit, and apply the principle of istihsan to distinguish between customary and non-customary conditions.

Core Idea
Istihsan allows valid sales with customary conditions, even if qiyas would void them.

In this lesson, we examine a specific sale transaction: selling a sandal with the condition that the seller makes it and shares a portion of the profit. By strict analogy (qiyas), such a condition would render the sale void because it is not harmonious with the contract. However, the Hanafi school employs istihsan (juristic preference) based on widespread custom (ta'amul) to uphold the validity of the sale. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying Hanafi contract law in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling and the Apparent Conflict
The passage states that selling a sandal with the condition that the seller makes it and shares the profit is valid by istihsan, but qiyas would deem it void. Note the tension between the strict analogy and the preferred ruling.
2
Understand the Qiyas Position
By qiyas, the condition is not harmonious with the contract (mula'im) because it introduces a benefit for the buyer that the contract does not require. This makes the contract void under general principles.
The asl is the general rule that conditions not mula'im invalidate a sale.
3
Recognize the Istihsan Argument
Istihsan overrides qiyas because of established custom (ta'amul) among people. The custom is widespread and provides benefit, so the ruling is changed to validity.
Custom must be widespread and not contradict an explicit text.
4
Compare with the Garment Example
The passage contrasts the sandal sale with a condition of sewing a garment. Since there is no such custom for garments, the original qiyas applies and the sale is void.
Distinguishes between customary and non-customary conditions.
5
Conclude the Operative Ruling
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that the sale of a sandal with the mentioned condition is valid due to istihsan. This is the position relied upon for fatwa.
The custom validates the condition, making the contract sahih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وصح بيع نعل على أن يحذوه ويشركه والقياس فساده) لما فيه من النفع للمشتري مع كون العقد لا يقتضيه وما ذكره جواب الاستحسان للتعامل وفي الخروج عن العادة خرج بين بخلاف اشتراط خياطة الثوب لعدم العادة فبقي على أصل القياس. وتسمير هذا التفسير وقد قدم في أول القولة قوله أي لم يجز بيع أمة بشرط منها وهو فاسد» قوله : «إن لم يدر العاقدان ذلك» قال الرملي : ولو
His saying: (And the sale of a sandal is valid on condition that he makes it and shares [the profit], and the qiyas is its voidness) because there is benefit for the buyer while the contract does not require it. And what he mentioned is the answer of istihsan due to custom. And in departing from custom, it becomes distinguished, unlike the condition of sewing a garment because there is no custom, so it remains on the original qiyas. And this interpretation of nailing? has been presented earlier in the first statement: his saying that it is not valid to sell a slave-woman with a condition from her, and it is void. His saying: 'If the two contractors do not know that' – al-Ramli said: and if...
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage concludes the section with a ruling on the sale of a sandal (or slipper) with the condition that the seller makes it and shares a portion of the profit with the buyer. By strict analogy (qiyas), such a condition is not harmonious with the contract (which requires immediate transfer) and would make the sale void. However, the Hanafi school applies istihsan (juristic preference) to validate this sale because of widespread custom ('urf) among people in such transactions. The condition benefits the buyer and is commonly practiced. In contrast, the condition of sewing a garment is not customary in this manner, so it remains on the original qiyas and the sale would be void. This shows how custom can override qiyas in Hanafi law when it is established and not contradictory to a clear text. The text then references an earlier statement about the sale of a slave-woman with a condition, indicating consistency in the legal framework.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; departing from qiyas due to custom or necessity.
تعامل (ta'amul): Customary practice among people; used as a basis for istihsan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school validate the sale of a sandal with a condition of making and sharing profit?
A) Because it is a necessary contract
B) Because of established custom (ta'amul) among people
C) Because the condition is explicitly mentioned in the Quran
D) Because it is a form of gift

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school validates a sale with a condition of making and sharing profit due to istihsan based on custom, distinguishing it from non-customary conditions that remain void by qiyas.

Exception in Sales: The Core Rule of Specification
Ruling on exception with and without specification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between valid and invalid exceptions in a sale contract based on whether the excepted item is specified.

Core Idea
An exception in a sale is only valid if the excepted item is individually identified; otherwise the sale is fasid.

The Hanafi school strictly regulates exceptions in sales to prevent uncertainty (jahalah). The key is that the excepted item must be known and distinguishable from the rest. This lesson walks through the foundational rule and its key example from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of exception
Look at the phrase 'except something'—is the excepted item a specific object or an unspecified quantity?
E.g., 'except a dirham' vs 'except this dirham'.
2
Check if the excepted item is specified (mu'ayyan)
If the item is individually pointed out or described so that it is identifiable, the exception is valid. Otherwise, it is ambiguous.
3
Apply the ruling
If specified, the sale is valid. If not specified, the sale is fasid due to jahalah leading to potential dispute.
The excepted item must not cause uncertainty about the sold quantity.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf differed on some cases; the mufta bihi is that specification makes it valid.
This is the relied-upon position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
استثناء قفيز من الصبرة على المعتمد. [...] ولو باع بألف دينار إلا درهماً أو إلا ثوباً أو إلا كر حنطة أو هذه الشياه إلا واحدة لا يجوز، ولو كانت بعينها جاز. ولو باع داراً على أن لا بناء فيها فإذا فيها بناء فالبيع فاسد لأنه يحتاج إلى نقض البناء. ولو باعها على أن بناءها من آجر فإذا هو لين فسد بناء على أنهما جنسان كما لو باعه ثوباً على أنه هروي فظهر بلخياً. ولو باع الأرض على أن فيها بناء فإذا لا بناء فيها أو اشتراها بشجرها فليس فيها شجر جاز وله الخيار وكذا لو باع بعلوها وسفلها فظهر أن لا علو لها ومثله لو اشترى بأجذاعها؛ كذا في فتح القدير .
Exception of a qafeez from a pile is upon the relied-upon opinion. [...] And if he sold for a thousand dinars except a dirham or except a garment or except a load of wheat or these sheep except one, it is not valid, and if [the excepted item] was specified, it is valid. And if he sold a house on condition that there is no building in it, and there is a building, the sale is invalid because it requires demolition. And if he sold it on condition that its building is of brick, and it is of soft brick, it is invalid on the basis that they are two different types, just as if he sold a garment on condition that it is Herawi and it turned out to be Balkhi. And if he sold land on condition that there is a building in it, and there is no building, or bought it with its trees and there are no trees, the sale is valid and he has the option; similarly if he sold with its upper part and lower part and it turned out there is no upper part; like if he bought with its date-palms; thus in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents numerous rulings on exceptions (istithna) and conditions in sale contracts. The author expands on the rule that an exception from a sale is only valid if the excepted item can be individually identified and separated. For example, excepting a known qafeez from a grain pile is valid, but excepting a sheep from an unspecified flock is not. However, if the sheep is pointed out, the exception becomes valid. Similarly, excepting a single dirham or garment from a lump sum sale without specification invalidates the sale, but specifying the item makes it valid. The passage then discusses conditions about building material and presence of structures. If a house is sold on condition it has no building but a building exists, the sale is invalid due to the need for demolition. If the condition is about the type of building material and it differs, the sale is likewise invalid as it is considered a material change. However, if the condition is about the presence of a building or trees and they are absent, the buyer has the option (khiyar) to annul or uphold the sale. The final examples address sales of upper and lower portions of a property. This follows Hanafi principles of jahalah (uncertainty) leading to fasād.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithnāʾ): Exception from a sale contract — the seller excludes a part of the sold item from the sale.
جهالة (jahālah): Uncertainty in a contract term that leads to dispute and invalidates the sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone sells a flock of sheep for 100 dirhams except one unspecified sheep, what is the ruling according to Hanafi fiqh?
A) The sale is valid, and the excepted sheep is considered unknown.
B) The sale is fasid because the excepted sheep is not specified.
C) The sale is batil without any possibility of correction.
D) The sale is valid if the buyer agrees to the exception.

Key Takeaway
For an exception in a sale to be valid, the excepted item must be individually specified; otherwise, the sale is fasid due to jahalah.

Conditions in Sales: Presence and Material of Structures
Rulings on conditions about building presence, material, and existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate the rulings when a condition about a building's material or its presence is unmet in a sale, and apply the ruling that gives the buyer the option (khiyar) when a condition about existence is false.

Core Idea
A condition about a building's material or its absence invalidates the sale if false; but a condition about its presence gives the buyer a choice to uphold or annul.

The passage continues with conditions attached to a sale. When the seller sets a condition that turns out to be false, the Hanafi school distinguishes between conditions regarding the quality of an item and conditions regarding its existence. The former invalidates the sale entirely; the latter grants the buyer the option of khiyar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of condition
Is the condition about the existence of something (e.g., there is a building) or about its nonexistence (e.g., no building)? Or about its quality (e.g., brick building)?
2
Determine if the condition is a material difference
If the condition concerns the material or type (e.g., Herawi vs Balkhi cloth), and it is false, the sale is invalid because the object is different from what was contracted.
This is treated as two different types of goods.
3
Apply the ruling for existence conditions
If the condition is that a building or trees exist, and they do not, the sale is not invalid per se; instead, the buyer has the option (khiyar) to cancel or affirm the sale.
This is because the absence does not change the essence of the sold item.
4
Apply the ruling for nonexistence conditions
If the condition is that there is no building, and a building exists, the sale is invalid because rectifying it would require demolition.
Similarly, if the condition is about the material and it differs, the sale is invalid (fasid).

Source Text

Key Terms
خيار (khiyār): Option or choice given to a buyer when a condition is not met; the buyer may annul or affirm the sale.
نقض (naqḍ): Demolition or dismantling; here, the need to demolish the existing building makes the sale invalid.

Validity of Selling a Sandal with a Condition of Cutting and Sharing
Explains istihsan ruling on selling sandals with conditions unlike tailoring.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling a sandal with a condition of cutting and sharing, explain why istihsan overrides qiyas based on custom, and distinguish it from a similar condition for tailoring a garment.

Core Idea
Custom can override strict analogy in sales, but only where the custom is widespread and consistent.

In Hanafi fiqh, sales with conditions that provide additional benefits are generally invalid by analogy (qiyas) because they involve uncertainty (jahalah) or combine contracts. However, the Hanafi school permits such conditions when they are supported by widespread custom (urf). This lesson examines the classic case of selling a sandal with a condition that the seller cuts and shares the profits, and why it is valid while a similar condition for tailoring a garment is not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the disputed condition
What is being sold, and what condition is attached? The sale is of a sandal with the condition that the seller cuts the sandal and shares (presumably the profits or the product).
The condition is not part of the default contract; it adds an obligation on the seller.
2
State the ruling by strict analogy (qiyas)
By strict analogy, such a condition introduces uncertainty (jahalah) regarding the object or price, or combines two contracts in one, which is prohibited. Therefore, qiyas would invalidate the sale.
This is the default rule before considering custom.
3
Explain the istihsan exception based on custom
The Hanafi school accepts istihsan, which prefers a ruling that is more suitable. Here, the widespread custom of sandal makers in conducting such sales removes the uncertainty and makes the condition acceptable. Thus, the sale is valid.
Custom (urf) must be consistent and widely practiced among the relevant tradespeople.
4
Compare with the tailoring case
The text distinguishes this from a condition to tailor a garment, because no widespread custom exists among tailors to sell unfinished fabric with a tailoring condition. Hence, the qiyas ruling of invalidity remains.
If a similar custom develops among tailors, the ruling may change.
5
Understand the underlying principle
Custom can eliminate uncertainty (jahalah) in known trades, allowing conditions that would otherwise invalidate a sale. This is an application of the Hanafi legal maxim: 'al-urf muhakkam' (custom is authoritative).
Custom must be consistent and not contradict a clear text or established principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وصح بيع نعل على أن يحذوه ويشركه والقياس فساده) لما فيه من النفع للمشتري مع كون العقد لا يقتضيه وما ذكره جواب الاستحسان للتعامل وفي الخروج عن العادة خرج بين بخلاف اشتراط خياطة الثوب لعدم العادة فبقي على أصل القياس. وتسمير هذا التفسير وقد قدم في أول القولة قوله أي لم يجز بيع أمة بشرط منها وهو فاسد» قوله : (أو هذه الشياه هذه المسألة مكررة بما مر آنفاً قول المصنف : إن لم يدر العاقدان ذلك) قال الرملي : ولو البيع إلى النيروز والمهرجان وصوم النصارى وفطر اليهود إن لم يدر العاقدان ذلك وإلى قدوم القبقاب كتشريك النعل كما في فتح القدير. وفي البزازية : اشترى ثوباً أو خفاً خلقاً على أن يرقعه البائع ويخرزه ويسلمه صح للعرف ومعنى يحذوه يقطعه
His statement: (And the sale of a sandal is valid on condition that he cuts it and shares with him, while analogy requires its invalidity) because there is benefit for the buyer while the contract does not necessitate it, and what he mentioned is the answer of istihsan due to customary practice, and by departing from custom, [the ruling] came out, unlike the condition of tailoring a garment due to lack of custom, so it remained on the original analogy. And the interpretation of this has been presented earlier in the first statement: his statement: 'It is not permissible to sell a slave-girl with a condition on her, and it is fasid.' His statement: (Or these sheep: this issue is repeated from what has passed) the author's statement: 'If the two contracting parties do not know that.' Al-Ramli said: And the sale to Nawruz and Mihrajan and the fast of Christians and the breaking of fast of Jews if the two contracting parties do not know that, and to the arrival of the clog like sharing the sandal, as in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Bazzaziyya: He bought a worn garment or khuff on condition that the seller patches it and sews it and delivers it; it is valid due to custom. And the meaning of 'yahdhuwahu' is 'he cuts it'.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on selling a sandal with a condition that the seller cuts and shares profits, which is an exception to the general rule. By strict analogy (qiyās), such a condition would invalidate the sale because it adds a benefit for the buyer not inherent in the contract. However, istihsān permits it based on customary practice ('urf) among sandal makers. The author distinguishes this from tailoring a garment, where no such custom exists, so analogy remains. The text then references earlier statements about selling a slave-girl with conditions and repetition of the sheep example. Al-Ramli adds that selling with deferred payment to festivals like Nawruz and Mihrajan is invalid if the parties do not know the dates, similar to the sandal sharing condition. Finally, al-Bazzaziyya reports that buying a worn garment or shoe on condition that the seller repairs it is valid due to custom, emphasizing that customs can overrule qiyās in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
استحسان (istiḥsān): Preferring a ruling that is more suitable, often based on custom, over strict analogy.
عارفة (ʿārifah): Custom ('urf) that validates certain conditions in sale contracts.
نعل (naʿl): Sandal or shoe; the object of sale in this case.
يحذوه (yaḥdhuwahu): He cuts it; refers to cutting the sandal to shape or size.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is selling a sandal with the condition that the seller cuts and shares profits valid according to Hanafi fiqh, while a similar condition for tailoring a garment is not?
A) Because sandals are more expensive than garments.
B) Because there is a widespread custom among sandal makers, unlike tailors.
C) Because tailoring is a separate contract.
D) Because the sandal condition benefits the seller more.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a valid custom (urf) can override strict analogy and validate conditions in sales that would otherwise be invalid.

Selling with Deferred Payment to Unknown Festivals: Jahalah Invalidates
Explanation of jahalah as cause of invalidity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on deferred sale to unknown festivals and distinguish when the sale is valid based on knowledge of the term.

Core Idea
A sale deferred to an unknown festival is fasid because uncertainty leads to dispute.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq explains why deferring payment to festivals like Nawruz (spring equinox) or Mihrajan (autumnal equinox) renders a sale invalid unless both parties know the exact date. The key legal reason (illah) is jahalah (uncertainty) in the term, which leads to potential disputes. Understanding this ruling helps you apply the principle that contractual certainty is foundational in Hanafi muamalat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a sale with deferred payment tied to a festival like Nawruz or Mihrajan. The ruling is that the sale is fasid (corrupt) if the date is unknown.
Fasid means the contract is legally defective and must be rectified or rescinded.
2
Locate the dalil
The passage implies a hadith prohibiting such sales, but the primary evidence is the principle that jahalah leads to dispute, which is a general prohibition in the Sunnah.
The hadith is taken as guidance, not as a direct command, because it is ahad.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition is based on the illah of jahalah. If the term is unknown, the sale is invalid. The passage also gives an exception: if the term is known (e.g., the end of Christian fast as they fast specific days), the sale is valid.
The exception confirms that the illah is jahalah, not the festival itself.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Ibn Kamal Pasha in al-Islah holds that if only one party knows the exact date, the sale is also invalid because ignorance of one party is enough to cause dispute.
Al-Ramli prefers the position that both must know, making the mufta bihi stricter.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under the stronger view, even if the seller knows the date, the buyer's ignorance still invalidates. This protects against exploitation.
The mufta bihi position is that jahalah of either party invalidates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لا البيع إلى النيروز والمهرجان وصوم النصارى وفطر اليهود إن لم يدر العاقدان ذلك أي لا يجوز البيع وهو فاسد لجهالة الأجل وهي مفضية إلى المنازعة في البيع لابتنائها على المماكسة إلا إذا كانا يعرفانه لكونه معلوماً عندهما أو كان التأجيل إلى فطر النصارى بعدما شرعوا في صومهم بالأيام لأن صومهم بالأيام معلوم فلا جهالة فيه والنيروز أول يوم من الصيف وهو أول يوم تحل فيه الشمس الحمل. والمهرجان أول يوم من الشتاء وهو أول يوم تحل فيه الشمس الميزان؛ كذا في السراج الوهاج . ثم قال : وإنما خص الصوم بالنصارى والفطر باليهود لأن غير معلوم وفطرهم معلوم واليهود بعكسه مع أنه إذا باع إلى صوم اليهود فالحكم كذلك لا الشتاء مبني على صوم النصارى دراه أحدهما ولم يدر الآخر فكذلك لا يجوز لإفضائه إلى المنازعة وعبارة الإصلاح لابن كمال باشا إن لم يعرف أحدهما ذلك اهـ . والعبارة الخالية من النقد إن لم يدريا أو أحدهما تأمل. قوله: (والنيروز أول يوم من الصيف الخ قال في النهر : هذا إنما يتم بناء على أن الربيع من الصيف والخريف من الشتاء وقد مر في الصلاة نظيره وإلا فالفصول أربعة كما لا يخفى وقيل : هما عيدان للمجوس اهـ . وذكر قبله النيروز أول يوم من طرف الربيع تحل فيه الشمس برج الحمل والمهرجان يوم في طرف الخريف وهو أول يوم من الشتاء تحل فيه الشمس الميزان .اهـ. ولا يخفى أن قوله «وهو أول يوم من أن الخريف من الشتاء وإلا فأول فصل الشتاء أول . هو يوم تحل فيه الشمس في الجدي فلو أسقطه لكان أولى تأمل. وفي القهستاني : النيروز أنواع نيروز العامة وهو أول يوم من فرد مين ماه، ونيروز الخاصة وهو النيروز الخاص، ونيروز السلطان وهو أول يوم يكون في نصف نهار والشمس في أول درجة من درجات الحمل، ونيروز المجوس ويقال له نيروز الدهاقين وهو اليوم الذي تحل فيه الشمس في الحوت والمهرجان نوعان: عامة وهو أول يوم من الخريف أعني اليوم السادس عشر من مهرماه وخاصة وهو اليوم السادس والعشرون منه .اهـ. قوله : (ثم قال الخ) قال الرملي : لا يخفى على ذي فهم أن قوله في المتن إن لم يدر المتعاقدان ذلك تبعاً لما في غيره أن المدار على علم المتعاقدين لا غير لأخذ الجهالة علة في الفساد والحكم يدور معها كيفما دارت، فيجب أن يكون النيروز والمهرجان وصوم النصارى وفطرهم وفطر اليهود وصومهم سواء في ذلك تأمل. لأن مع قوله قوله : (مع أنه إذا باع إلى صوم اليهود فالحكم كذلك أي أن علماه صح وإلا فلا وتأمله صوم النصارى غير معلوم «الخ. وفي القهستاني : وصوم النصارى سبعة وثلاثون يوماً في مدة ثمانية وأربعين يوماً فإن ابتداء صومهم يوم الاثنين الذي يكون قريباً من اجتماع النيرين الواقع ثاني شباط وثامن آذار ولا يصومون يوم الأحد ولا يوم السبت إلا يوم السبت الثامن والأربعين، ويكون فطرهم يعني يوم عيدهم يوم الأحد بعد ذلك. وفطر اليهود أن يأكلوا سبعة أيام من خامس عشر من البحر الرائق/ ج ٦/ م١٠
His statement: (Do not sell to Nawruz, Mihrajan, the fast of Christians, and the breaking of fast of Jews if the two contracting parties do not know that) meaning the sale is not valid and it is fasid due to jahalah in the term, which leads to dispute in sale because it is based on bargaining, unless they both know it because it is known to them, or if the deferment is to the breaking of the fast of Christians after they have begun their fast by days because their fast is by days so there is no jahalah. And Nawruz is the first day of summer, and it is the first day the sun enters Aries. And Mihrajan is the first day of winter, and it is the first day the sun enters Libra; thus in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. Then he said: And he only specified the fast for Christians and the breaking of fast for Jews because [the former] is unknown and their breaking of fast is known, and for Jews it is the opposite; and if he sold to the fast of Jews, the ruling is likewise. ... And in al-Qahistani: Nawruz has types: the public Nawruz is the first day of Farvardin Mah, the special Nawruz is the special Nawruz, the Sultan's Nawruz is the first day at noon when the sun is at the first degree of Aries, and the Magian Nawruz is the day the sun enters Pisces. Mihrajan has two types: the public is the first day of autumn, the sixteenth of Mihr Mah, and the special is the twenty-sixth. ... And in al-Qahistani: The fast of Christians is thirty-seven days in a period of forty-eight days, starting on Monday near the conjunction of the two lights on February 2 and March 8, they do not fast Sundays or Saturdays except the 48th Saturday, and their break fast is Sunday after that. The break of fast of Jews is eating seven days from the fifteenth of [March?].
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi ruling on selling with deferred payment tied to festivals like Nawruz (Persian spring equinox) and Mihrajan (autumnal equinox), or to the fasting period of Christians or breaking of fast of Jews. The key issue is jahalah (uncertainty) about the exact date. If neither party knows when the term occurs, the sale is fasid because the uncertainty leads to potential disputes. However, if both know the term, it is valid. An exception is made for deferment to the Christian breaking of fast after they have begun fasting, because their fast consists of known days. The passage provides astronomical definitions of Nawruz and Mihrajan as seasonal markers, though some scholars note that these are based on older classifications where spring and autumn are part of summer and winter. Al-Qahistani gives detailed types of Nawruz and Mihrajan based on Persian calendar. Al-Ramli clarifies that the criterion is the knowledge of the contracting parties, not the nature of the festival itself; thus all such deferments should be treated equally. The passage ends with specific details of Christian fasting periods (seven weeks starting from a Monday near Feb 2 and March 8) and the Jewish feast of unleavened bread.

Key Terms
نيروز (nayrūz): The first day of summer/spring equinox, a Persian festival.
مهرجان (mihrajān): The first day of winter/autumnal equinox, a Persian festival.
جهالة (jahālah): Uncertainty or ignorance about a essential term of a contract, leading to invalidity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, when is selling with deferred payment to Nawruz valid?
A) It is never valid because Nawruz is a non-Islamic festival.
B) It is valid if both the buyer and seller know the exact date of Nawruz.
C) It is only valid if the seller knows the date.
D) It is valid if the price is increased to compensate for uncertainty.

Key Takeaway
A sale deferred to a festival is valid only if both parties know the exact date at the time of contract, because uncertainty invalidates the term.
